

2018 WL 2761416 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SATURNINO ELIAS PINEDA-VIDES
File: AXXX XX9 992 - Harlingen, TX
March 30, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Harry Asatrian, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa Putnam
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 24, 2017, denying his motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal. The respondent was previously ordered removed in absentia for failure to appear for his removal hearing on April 19, 2006.1 See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). In his appeal, the respondent also requests that the proceedings be reopened and the case be remanded to the Immigration Court based on ineffective assistance by his prior attorney. We will construe the requests as a motion to remand. See Correa-Rivera v. Holder, 706 F.3d 1128, 1131 (9th Cir. 2013) (construing an appeal asserting an ineffective assistance claim as a motion to reopen); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4). The appeal will be dismissed and the motion to remand will be denied.
 
We review Immigration Judges’ findings of fact for clear error. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent claims ineffective assistance against an attorney who filed a motion to reopen on his behalf with the Immigration Court in Harlingen, Texas. Specifically, the respondent avers that the prior attorney performed ineffectively when he did not argue in the motion that the respondent’s failure to appear for his hearing was because he relied on erroneous advice from a notario (Respondent’s Br. at 7-9).2 The respondent further claims that he is prima facie eligible for asylum and that he would have pursued his asylum claim, but for his prior attorney’s failure to submit an application for asylum and withholding of removal, along with evidence of changed country conditions (Respondent’s Br. at 6, 15-16).
 
A motion to remand for the purpose of presenting additional evidence must conform to the same standards as a motion to reopen. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471-72 (BIA 1992). The instant motion to remand is untimely, having been filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia on April 19, 2006. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). It is also numerically barred as the respondent has already filed one motion to reopen with the Harlingen Immigration Court. See section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (BIA 2000) (holding that because the Immigration Judge entered a final administrative decision, the respondent’s motion to remand must be subject to the time and numerical limits for motions to reopen).
 
*2 Assuming, arguendo, that the instant motion was not untimely or numerically barred, the respondent is not entitled to reopening based on ineffective assistance by the prior attorney because he has not demonstrated that the ineffective representation, if any, resulted in prejudice. See Miranda-Lores v. I.N.S., 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994) (requiring an alien to show “ineffective representation” and “substantial prejudice” as a result of the ineffective representation when raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 638 (requiring the respondent to demonstrate that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance when making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).3
 
The respondent asserts that his prior attorney rendered ineffective assistance when she did not argue that his failure to appear was due to misinformation from a notario (Respondent’s Br. at 7). See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and 240(e)(1) of the Act; see also Galvez-Vergara v. Gonzales, 484 F.3d 798, 802 (5th Cir. 2007) (determining that advice of an attorney not to appear at an immigration proceeding constitutes an exceptional circumstance beyond an alien’s control). In refuting this claim, the prior attorney states in her response to the respondent’s allegations that “My records show that [the respondent] advised me that he had consulted with an attorney and that attorney had asked him to come back to his office in three weeks if he did not get any court notice in the mail. The attorney told him that he could not represent him in court if there is no court date yet. I do not think there are genuine grounds for an ineffective assistance of counsel under these circumstances” (Respondent’s Br. at 13). As such, the respondent has not demonstrated that his prior attorney performed ineffectively.
 
There was also no substantial prejudice to the respondent as his motion was untimely and the filing deadline would not have been equitably tolled because the respondent did not demonstrate due diligence in pursuing his case. Even if the respondent’s prior attorney had successfully argued the exceptional circumstance of ineffective assistance, his motion to reopen was filed well beyond 180 days after the date of the order of removal on April 19, 2006. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i).
 
Moreover, the filing deadline would not have been equitably tolled because the respondent did not establish due diligence in pursuing his rights or any extraordinary circumstance that prevented him from timely filing. Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir. 2016); see Toora v. Holder, 603 F.3d 282, 284 (5th Cir. 2010) (finding that equitable tolling did not excuse the respondent’s late-filed motion because the respondent failed to exercise due diligence in seeking a rescission of his deportation order). The respondent filed his first motion to reopen 11 years after he was ordered removed in absentia on April 19, 2006. Furthermore, he sought legal assistance only after he was detained by the Department of Homeland Security pursuant to a traffic stop (Respondent’s Br. at 2). Under these circumstances, the prior attorney’s conduct did not prejudice the respondent as his motion to reopen was untimely.
 
*3 While the respondent’s prior attorney did not submit an asylum application or evidence of changed country conditions to the Immigration Judge with the motion to reopen, this error also did not prejudice the respondent. In this case, proving prejudice requires the respondent to make a prima facie showing that he may have been entitled to asylum or withholding of removal had the prior attorney performed effectively. See Miranda-Lores v. I.N.S., 17 F.3d at 85 (holding that the petitioner was not prejudiced by his counsel’s ineffective assistance where he did not merit the grant of relief). Yet, even taking the respondent’s representations as true for purposes of his motion, his fear of gang violence and extortion in El Salvador would not qualify him for relief. Castillo-Enriquez v. Holder, 690 F. 3d 667(5th Cir. 2012) (holding that fear of extortion based on perceived wealth is not a ground for asylum); Matter of M-E-V-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 227, 250 (observing that extortion and other violent gang behavior do not, by themselves, establish a basis for asylum). See also Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161, 169 (BIA 2013) (requiring an alien seeking reopening to establish a prima facie case for a grant asylum); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. at 472 (holding that the Board may deny a motion to reopen where a prima facie case for the relief sought has not been established).
 
Finally, the respondent argues in the alternative that we should exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen the case (Respondent’s Br. at 16-17). However, we conclude that there are no exceptional circumstances that would warrant reopening and remanding this matter pursuant to our sua sponte authority under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Edward F. Kelly
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The removal proceedings were conducted in absentia under section 240(b)(5) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), which applies to cases in which service of the hearing notice occurs after April 1, 1997.

2

In the motion to reopen, the prior attorney initially claimed that the respondent did not appear for his removal hearing because he did not receive notice of his hearing date (Respondent’s Mot. at 362). However, on appeal, the respondent conceded that he was properly served the Notice to Appear (NTA), which contained the hearing date and the consequences of failing to appear (Respondent’s Br. at 7). Therefore, the Immigration Judge did not err in finding that there was proper notice to the respondent (IJ at 1).

3

We will consider the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim against the prior attorney because the respondent meets the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998) (Respondent’s Mot. at 32-64). See Hernandez-Ortez v. Holder, 741 F.3d 644, 647 (5th Cir. 2014) (holding that an alien must strictly comply with the Lozada requirements); see also Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639 (setting forth the three requirements that a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must meet).

2018 WL 2761442 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUNIOR JOSUE MEJIA GALINDO A.K.A. JUNIOR MEJIA GALINDO
File: AXXX XX4 510 - Chicago, IL
March 23, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Heather Hadi, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on December 13, 2017, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
In the motion, the respondent argues that his proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Brian Bennet, who represented him at hearing and on appeal. Specifically, the respondent argues that the former counsel provided ineffective assistance because the former counsel: (1) sought a continuance of the respondent’s proceedings to pursue post-conviction relief of one of the respondent’s convictions, even though the respondent would still be ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), under the “stop time rule,” even if the post-conviction relief were granted; and (2) failed to advise the respondent to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture. The respondent’s motion states that he filed a bar complaint against the former counsel and notified the former counsel of the ineffective assistance of counsel claim (Motion;1 Motion Exhs. B-C).
 
As a threshold matter, despite the arguments that the respondent filed a bar complaint and notified the former counsel of the ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the respondent has not shown that he has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The document submitted as proof of filing a bar complaint appears to be only part of the necessary complaint form (“Details of Complaint”) and the respondent did not submit evidence that the complaint was actually sent to the bar authority (Motion Exh. B). Furthermore, this complaint statement, which appears to be from the respondent’s mother, states that the former counsel “has not done anything for my son.” (id.). The brief email communication from current counsel to former counsel only addressed the respondent’s ineligibility for cancellation of removal, but did not address the alleged failure by the former counsel to advise the respondent to apply for asylum and related forms of relief (Motion Exh. C). Also significantly, the respondent has not submitted his own affidavit regarding the representation by the former counsel.2 Since the respondent did not substantially comply with Matter of Lozada, the motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel claim will be denied.
 
*2 In any event, the respondent has not shown prejudice from the ineffective assistance of counsel. While the respondent argues that the former counsel provided ineffective assistance for seeking continuance, he has not claimed or shown that the outcome of his proceedings was potentially affected because he sought a continuance. See El-Gazawy v. Holder, 690 F.3d 852, 860 (7th Cir. 2012); Rapheal v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 521, 533 (7th Cir. 2008) (prejudice means that the lack of a fair hearing actually had the potential for affecting the outcome of the proceedings); Hamid v. Gonzales, 417 F.3d 642, 646-47 (7th Cir. 2005) (applicant’s failure to allege excluded testimony that would potentially affect outcome of hearing was fatal to due process claim).
 
As to the former counsel’s alleged failure to apply for asylum and related forms of relief, the respondent submitted letters from his mother and sister stating that they have received threats from people in Honduras who are now living on the land owned by the respondent and his sister, and that a cousin was killed in February 2017 while trying to protect land (Motion Exhs. D-E). However, the claim of threats shown in these documents is only general and vague, and the respondent’s motion offers no analysis on how he may have been granted asylum or related forms of relief based on the facts alleged.
 
Based on the above, the respondent’s motion will be denied. The motion for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s motion was not paginated as required in Board of Immigration Appeals Practice Manual, § 3.3(c)(iii) (“Briefs and other submission should always be paginated”).

2

The motion states that “Due to the immediate need for this motion to be filed all documents have not been attained such as . . . an affidavit from the [r]espondent” (Motion). However, the motion does not state that the respondent’s affidavit would be submitted when it becomes available, nor has any further evidence been submitted.

2018 WL 2761453 (BIA)

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TAOHEED OLABIYI AJAO A.K.A. TAOHEED AJAO A.K.A. MICHAEL SMITH
File: AXXX XX1 694 - Dallas, TX
March 19, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaime Jasso, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on November 20, 2017, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen so that he may present additional evidence relating to his allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion is also in the nature of a motion to reconsider. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The motion and request for a stay of removal will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent’s motion is in the nature of both a motion to reconsider and a motion to reopen. A motion to reconsider contests the correctness of the original decision based upon the previous factual record, whereas a motion to reopen seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57-58 (BIA 2006). A party seeking reconsideration requests that the original decision be reexamined in light of alleged legal or factual errors, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. See O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. at 56; Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). Here, the respondent challenges the decision of the Immigration Judge and the Board and seeks to submit additional evidence in support of his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
We first address that aspect of the motion requesting reconsideration. Any motion to reconsider a decision of the Board must be filed within 30 days after the date of that decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). See section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(B). The record reflects that the Board did not receive the motion until February 8, 2018. The motion to reconsider was therefore filed untimely. Additionally, even if we reached the merits of the motion, it does not assert any error of fact or law in the Board’s decision, but essentially reiterates his arguments regarding his removability. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. at 56 (discussing regulatory requirement that a motion to reconsider specify “errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision”); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The Board considered these arguments and ultimately found them unpersuasive when we dismissed his appeal. Disagreement with the result, or raising arguments that could have been made previously, is not sufficient. Therefore, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
*2 We turn next to the timely motion to reopen. Applicable regulations provide that a motion to reopen “shall state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Moreover, a movant must satisfy the “heavy burden” of establishing that if the proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). To prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien must show that competent counsel would have acted otherwise and that the alien was prejudiced. See Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent still has not substantially complied with the Lozada procedural requirements.1 While the respondent has submitted a letter drafted to his former attorney, there is no objective evidence that this letter has actually been sent to or served on the respondent’s former attorney (Motion at page 10). More importantly, there is no evidence that a bar complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Regardless, the respondent still has not established prejudice. As mentioned earlier, none of the respondent’s arguments persuades us of any error in our prior determination that the respondent was properly placed in removal proceedings as an “arriving alien” and that he is removable as charged. Thus, the respondent has not demonstrated prejudice from counsel’s actions or omissions such that he has established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. As such, the respondent’s motion is insufficient to meet his “heavy burden” of showing that it is likely that the result would change if the proceedings were reopened. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464. Therefore, we do not find that he has met his burden of showing that reopening of these proceedings is warranted.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Matter of Lozada, 19 I & N Dec. at 637, requires a successful ineffective assistance of counsel claim be accompanied by: “(1) an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement with former counsel concerning what action would be taken and what counsel did or did not represent in this regard; (2) proof that the alien notified former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance and allowed counsel an opportunity to respond; and (3) if a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities is claimed, a statement as to whether the alien filed a complaint with any disciplinary authority regarding counsel’s conduct and, if a complaint was not filed, an explanation for not doing so.”).

2018 WL 2761465 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HILARIA BRAMBILA-PELAYO
File: AXXX XX8 528 - Seattle, WA
March 14, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ji Min Kim, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Xiao Yan Huang
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The proceedings in this matter were last before the Board on June 9, 2003, at which time the Board affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge of January 3, 2002. By a motion filed on October 20, 2017, the respondent seeks to have these proceedings reopened. The Department of Homeland Security has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
By the motion now before the Board, the respondent asserts a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in order to seek the reopening of proceedings. The attorney who represented the respondent in those proceedings was disbarred in 2012.
 
The Board may in some cases grant a motion to reopen, including an untimely motion, in order to remedy an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See generally Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-640 (BIA 1988). However, it is incumbent upon the affected party to show that he or she exercised due diligence in pursuit of his or her claim and to comply generally with the requirements set forth in Lozada. See Ghahremani v. Gonzales, 498 F.3d 993 (9th Cir. 2007) (equitable tolling recognized when a petitioner is prevented from filing because of error as long as the petitioner acts with due diligence); Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672 (9th Cir. 2011).
 
Given the passage of more than 14 years since our decision in this case, and the respondent’s failure to meaningfully show that she exercised due diligence in pursuing her ineffective assistance of counsel claim, we find no basis for tolling the time limits for motions in this case. Furthermore, the respondent has not shown that counsel’s representation was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Maravilla-Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855 (9th Cir. 2004). Contrary to the arguments presented in the motion, the respondent was at least aware of the order of removal against her, and the reasons for the Immigration Judge’s denial of a further continuance (her own father’s failure to file an N-400 naturalization application or to even appear in court), as she was present with counsel and an interpreter at her January 2002 hearing. Furthermore, the respondent would not have been eligible for cancellation of removal at that time as she did not have 10 years of continuous physical presence. Absent such a showing, the respondent has failed to show the prejudice requisite to establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004) (to prevail on a due process claim founded on ineffective assistance of counsel, petitioner must demonstrate substantial prejudice resulting from the ineffectiveness).
 
*2 Finally, the respondent has not shown the existence of an exceptional situation warranting the grant of this untimely motion in the exercise of our sua sponte authority. While the respondent seeks to have the proceedings reopened in order to pursue adjustment of status based upon a petition filed on her behalf by her United States citizen son, untimely motions to reopen to pursue an application for adjustment of status do not fall within any of the statutory or regulatory exceptions to the time limits for motions to reopen before the Board and will generally be denied. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, we decline to reopen the proceedings in this matter and the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is, hereby, denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 2761465 (BIA)

2018 WL 2761487 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ISAAC EUSEBIO ORTIZ-SALAZAR A.K.A. ISAAC ELSALVADOR ORTIZ A.K.A. ISAAC EUSEBIO ORTIZSALAZAR A.K.A. ISAAC ORTIZ-SALAZAR A.K.A. ISAAC ELSALVADOR SALAZAR A.K.A. ISAAC EUSEBIO ORTIZ A.K.A. ISAAC EUSEBIO SALAZAR A.K.A. ISAAC EUSEBIO SALZAR A.K.A. ISAACEUSEBRO EUSEBIO SALAZAR A.K.A. ORTIZ EUSEBIO SALAZAR
File: AXXX XX4 494 - Atlanta, GA
March 8, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Emmanuel West, Esquire
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Panama, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated October 10, 2017, ordering his removal from the United States. To resolve any issues regarding the effectiveness of the respondent’s waiver of his appeal rights, we will consider this matter on appeal pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c). We will dismiss the appeal.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. At the removal hearing on October 10, 2017, the respondent, through counsel, conceded that he is subject to removal from the United States because, after his admission to this country as a nonimmigrant visitor in 2005, he did not timely depart (Tr. at 5; Exh. 1). Section 237(a)(1)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B). Through counsel, the respondent advised the Immigration Judge that “we have nothing to apply for that the court can grant” (Tr. at 4).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that, despite his prior counsel’s statements, he may qualify for cancellation of removal, asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture. However, the respondent is bound by his prior counsel’s concessions and has not complied with the evidentiary requirements needed to properly present a claim to ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). Additionally, given that the respondent has not presented an application for relief on appeal, nor fully articulated his claims, he has not established that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s decision to not pursue relief from removal in these proceedings. Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005).
 
Finally, on appeal, the respondent raises arguments regarding mental competency. However, he has not articulated any particular reason to believe that he, himself, lacks mental competency. See Matter of J-S-S-, 26 I&N Dec. 679, 680 (BIA 2015) (discussing a case where an alien had a long history of mental illness); Matter of M-A-M-, 25 I&N Dec. 474, 475 (BIA 2011). While, on appeal, the respondent argues that he did not understand what transpired in the courtroom, he does not argue that he was unable to meaningfully consult with his attorney. The mere fact that, due to a lack of fluency in the English language, the use of legal terms, or otherwise, an alien does not immediately understand the significance of an attorney’s decision to concede removability and decline to apply for relief does not, in itself, amount to an indicia of mental incompetency. Overall, the respondent has not raised sufficient claims on appeal which indicate that he lacks the capacity to understand the nature and object of the proceedings against him, to consult with counsel, and to assist in preparing his defense.
 
*2 For the reasons set forth above, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to order the respondent’s removal from the United States to Panama. The following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 2761487 (BIA)
End of Document

2018 WL 2761499 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HERMINIA ROSADO
File: AXXX XX7 151 - Newark, NJ
March 5, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jerard A. Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative order of removal in the respondent’s case on February 5, 2008, and on October 7, 2014, the Board denied the respondent’s second untimely motion to reopen for having been filed beyond the time and number limits imposed on motions to reopen. On August 8, 2017, the respondent filed the instant motion seeking reopening to enable her to apply for adjustment of status based on a visa petition, approved on August 25, 2011, classifying her as the parent of a United States citizen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not joined in or responded to this motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The motion is untimely and number barred, and the respondent has not shown that any exception to the filing deadline applies or should be applied in her case. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) (2017); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), (3) (2017). The respondent specifically seeks the exercise of the Board’s discretion to reopen this case sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The respondent emphasizes her equities accrued in the United States, and asserts that her family will suffer hardship as a result of her removal.
 
While we recognize that the respondent’s removal will likely result in hardship, the motion does not demonstrate an exceptional situation to warrant the exercise of our limited sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that “[t]he power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). In this regard, we note that becoming potentially eligible for relief after having been ordered removed from the United States is not an “exceptional situation”.
 
To the extent that the respondent alleges ineffective assistance of counsel with regard to the filing of the prior motions, she has not complied with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and ineffective assistance of counsel is not apparent on the face of the record. See Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 133 (3d Cir. 2001). The respondent alleges that in filing the prior motions to reopen for adjustment of status on her behalf, former counsel failed to submit supporting documentation, and erred with regard to a specific date and the spelling of her son’s name. The prior motions, however, were not denied because of such errors. Instead, they were denied because they exceeded the statutory and regulatory limits imposed on motions to reopen. Moreover, the instant motion does not demonstrate that the respondent suffered prejudice as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel as she has not shown that she was prima facie eligibility for any form of relief during the period within which a timely motion to reopen could have been filed. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 640. See Fadiga v. Attorney General of the United States, 488 F.3d 142, 157 (3d Cir. 2007). Hence, equitable tolling of the filing restrictions imposed on the instant motion to reopen is not warranted in this case. See Alzaarir v. Attorney General of the United States, 639 F.3d 86 (3d Cir. 2011).
 
*2 For the foregoing reasons, the respondent’s untimely, number-barred motion to reopen will be denied.1
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent, who is removable under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i) (alien present without being admitted or paroled), no longer needs to seek reopening of her removal proceedings to apply either to the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) for a provisional unlawful presence waiver or to the DHS for a stay of removal. Individuals with final orders of removal may apply for provisional waivers if they have first filed a Form I-212 application seeking consent to reapply for admission and such an application has been conditionally approved. 8 C.F.R. 212.7(e)(4)(iv). The USCIS has exclusive jurisdiction. 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(1). The respondent may request an administrative stay of removal from the DHS while any waiver or provisional waiver applications are pending before the USCIS. 8 C.F.R. § 241.6(a).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELMER JOEL MONTOYA-SANDOVAL
File: AXXX XX6 075 - Dallas, TX
March 1, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
W. John Vandenberg, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 26, 2017, denying his motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for his failure to appear for his hearing on May 21, 1998. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent contended that he did not receive the Notice to Appear (NTA) or the notice of hearing. See Respondent’s Motion at 1-2. Specifically, he admitted that shortly after entering the United States in 1998, he was placed into a “family detention center in Texas” and that after being transferred to another such facility, “he escaped.” See id. He therefore denied receipt of the NTA and hearing notices, and sought rescission of his removal order due to lack of notice.
 
On appeal, the respondent claims for the first time that he did not receive the notice of hearing because the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) “lost” him when he absconded from its custody shortly after being personally served with the NTA in April 1998. See Respondent’s Br. at 2-15. We also note that in his Notice of Appeal, the respondent claimed that the prior attorney who filed his motion to reopen provided ineffective assistance in failing to determine whether he had a fear of returning to Honduras, the country of his birth and to which he had been ordered removed.1 The respondent accordingly submits an application for asylum on appeal. See Respondent’s Br. at Exh. 6.
 
Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii), an order of removal entered in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal, if based on exceptional circumstances as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1). A motion to reopen based on a lack of notice under section 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act may be filed at any time. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Furthermore, the time and number limits for motions to reopen do not apply to a motion to reopen proceedings if the basis of the motion is to apply or reapply for asylum or withholding of removal based on changed country conditions or changed circumstances arising in the country of nationality, if such evidence is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(ii), 1003.23(b)(4)(i); see also Matter of J-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 161 (BIA 2013) (holding that proceedings conducted in absentia may be reopened where the alien seeks asylum and withholding of removal based on changed country conditions).
 
*2 Inasmuch as the record reflects that the NTA was served personally on the respondent on April 20, 1998,2 the Immigration Judge properly found that the respondent had notice of the initiation of these proceedings, and also of the requirement that he provide the DHS with his current address. See IJ at 2-3. An alien may not be entitled to receive notice of his hearing if he does not provide an address at which he may be contacted during proceedings. See section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Act (eliminating the notice requirement where the alien fails to provide the required contact information); see also Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d at 359 (court found that alien did not receive notice of his hearing because he failed to comply with his obligation to provide the Immigration Court with current address information); Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674-75 (BIA 2008) (stating that an alien must comply with the statutory responsibility to provide current address information). The respondent appears to argue on appeal that he never was under an obligation to update his address with the Immigration Court because (1) he was a minor, and (2) the DHS “lost” him and, therefore, assumed the obligation to somehow provide his current address to the court. See generally, Respondent’s Br. at 8-15. The respondent’s argument in this regard ignores the Immigration Judge’s factual finding (which lacks clear error) that he “escaped” the detention center, and in fact, was not “lost” due to the actions of the DHS. Further, a notice of the obligation to provide a current address, and the consequences of any failure to do so, was expressly set forth in the NTA with which the respondent was personally served. See Matter of Cubor, 25 I&N Dec. 470 (BIA 2011) (personal service of NTA on minor who is 14 years of age or older is effective). As such, any argument premised on the DHS failing to provide the basic standard of care to juvenile aliens in its custody fails.
 
To the extent that the respondent seeks reopening to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the Convention Against Torture, we reiterate that the time and number limits for motions to reopen do not apply if the motion is based on previously unavailable evidence of changed country conditions or changed circumstances arising in the country of nationality.3 We observe, however, that the respondent has not presented any documentation with his motion to reopen showing the country conditions in Honduras at the time that his removal order was issued on May 21, 1998, and thus has not shown that a material change has occurred since that time. The record also contains no sworn written statement by the respondent detailing his fears of returning to Honduras. See Waggoner v. Gonzales, 488 F.3d 632, 639 (5th Cir. 2007) (a motion to reopen must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documents); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). We therefore find inadequate support for the motion to reopen to permit the respondent to pursue this application for relief.
 
*3 Furthermore, although the respondent stated in his Notice of Appeal that the attorney who represented him in the filing of his motion to reopen provided ineffective assistance, this claim is not supported by a written statement detailing this claim, or by evidence that the respondent reported the alleged ineffective assistance to relevant state bar agencies or that he informed his prior counsel of the allegations leveled against him or gave him an opportunity to respond. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).
 
Finally, as a matter of discretion, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the facts presented in this case did not warrant an intervention of his sua sponte authority to reopen the proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In the appellate brief, the respondent’s attorney indicates that he was removed to Honduras on an unknown date. See Respondent’s Br. at 4. We have been unable to verify this contention via the administrative records of this agency.

2

The respondent’s NTA did not include the date and time of the hearing but the Immigration Judge found without clear error that it reflects that he was informed in English and Spanish of the consequences of failing to appear and of the need to update his address (IJ at 2). The NTA need not include the specific time and date of a removal hearing in order for the statutory notice requirements to be satisfied, and that information may be provided in a subsequent notice of hearing. See Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354, 358 (5th Cir. 2009).

3

This claim for relief was not raised in the motion to reopen but we will nevertheless address it since the respondent appears to argue that the failure to do so was based on ineffective assistance of counsel.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARNULFO DIEGO MARTINEZ A.K.A. ARNULFO MARTINEZ DIEGO
File: AXXX XX0 293 - Charlotte, North Carolina
February 28, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
H. Esteban Diaz, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Hilary Rainone
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s July 31, 2017, decision which denied his motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed a response in opposition. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was placed in removal proceedings with the issuance of a Notice to Appear on February 23, 2016 (Exh. 1). His counsel filed a motion to appear by telephone which was granted by the Immigration Judge on November 2, 2016, for all master calendar proceedings. The respondent appeared at a master calendar hearing on November 9, 2016, with counsel (via telephone), and conceded the charge of removability (IJ at 1). The respondent was instructed to file all applications for relief by the next hearing, which was scheduled for March 20, 2017, and he was advised that a failure to file any applications would constitute a waiver of these applications (IJ at 1).
 
On March 6, 2017, the respondent’s counsel filed a motion to continue, which was granted, and the hearing was continued until April 10, 2017. His counsel indicated in the motion that he had requested that the DHS exercise prosecutorial discretion to administratively close the proceedings.
 
On April 10, 2017, the respondent appeared with counsel (via telephone), he indicated that he would not file any applications for relief, he did not apply for pre-conclusion voluntary departure and he waived appeal (IJ at 1). The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed to Mexico in a preprinted minute order which included handwritten notations reflecting that the respondent “waived filing applications for relief and that counsel “concedes no appealable issues.”
 
On June 16, 2017, the respondent, through present counsel, filed a motion to reopen, along with an affidavit dated May 26, 2017. He requested reopening because he alleged that his prior counsel had committed ineffective assistance by not applying for cancellation of removal. The respondent attached a copy of a bar complaint filed with the North Carolina State Bar against his prior attorney dated May 26, 2017, and a May 31, 2017, letter from the bar indicating that his complaint had been received. The letter indicated therein that the bar “may also contact” his prior attorney for a response.
 
*2 On July 31, 2017, the Immigration Judge denied the motion. He noted that, although the respondent provided evidence that he filed a bar complaint against prior counsel, he had not established that his prior attorney had been notified of the complaint, or was provided an opportunity to respond (IJ at 2). Therefore, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent had not demonstrated his compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) to establish ineffective assistance (IJ at 2). In addition, he found that he had not met his burden to show prejudice because he was provided an opportunity to file any applications for relief, and declined (IJ at 2).
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994). The respondent made a strategic decision not to apply for cancellation of removal while his request for prosecutorial discretion was pending, and not to apply for voluntary departure at his hearing. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998) (discussing general rule of discouraging alien from revisiting prior tactical and strategic decisions). Furthermore, the respondent has not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, to establish ineffective assistance of counsel because he has not established that the state bar notified his prior attorney of his allegations, that his prior counsel was provided with an opportunity to respond, and he has not alleged or established that the actions of his prior counsel constituted egregious circumstances.1 See Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741, 746 (4th Cir. 2006) (“alien who fails to satisfy any of the three Lozada requirements will rarely, if ever, be in substantial compliance”); Figeroa v. INS, 886 F.2d 76 (4th Cir. 1989) (alien must show prejudice resulted from ineffective assistance).
 
The respondent’s May 26, 2017, affidavit is written in English. It is not accompanied by an original Spanish version or a certification of translation. However, the respondent’s brief on appeal indicated that Spanish is his native language and that he does not speak English. In addition, his appeal brief and affidavit indicated that he spoke with his prior attorney’s secretary both on the telephone and in person, and that the Immigration Judge asked him about his children during the second hearing. Moreover, the respondent conceded in his affidavit that the Immigration Judge informed him that he had waived appeal at his April 10, 2017, hearing. Therefore, it appears that either the respondent is able to speak and understand some English based on his presence in this country since 1997, or that he had the assistance of an interpreter during his interactions with his attorney’s office, the Immigration Judge, and when the May 26, 2017, affidavit was prepared.2
 
*3 To the extent that the respondent requested sua sponte reopening, he has not established an exceptional situation required for sua sponte reopening. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In this regard, the respondent’s counsel alleges in his brief on appeal that the North Carolina Rules of Professional Conduct suggest that the state bar policies resulted in a copy of the respondent’s complaint being forwarded to his prior attorney, who would have 15 days to respond (Respondent’s Brief at 4-5). However, this information is not contained in the record, counsel has not attached a copy of the state bar rules, and we decline to take administrative notice. In addition, counsel’s statement on appeal cannot constitute evidence. Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Finally, the May 31, 2017, letter from the state bar indicated that the bar may contact his prior attorney.

2

The respondent asserts in his affidavit that he does not want his two United States citizen children to move to Mexico. However, his United States citizen children are under no obligation to relocate to Mexico solely because the respondent has been ordered removed to Mexico. Matter of A-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 275 (BIA 2007). He indicates in his cancellation application that the mother of his two United States citizen children presently resides in Mexico.

IN RE: LIRAN TOLEDO
File: AXXX XX3 464 - Los Angeles, CA
February 27, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bashir Al-Nouri, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Israel, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of October 6, 2017, denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The Immigration Judge had earlier denied a motion to reopen removal proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia on September 26, 2012. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent’s July 27, 2017, motion to reconsider the November 14, 2012, denial of the motion to reopen was untimely. A motion for reconsideration must be filed within 30 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal. See section 240(c)(6) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). In addition, the Immigration Judge correctly found the motion, if considered a motion to reopen, is numerically barred, because the respondent had filed a previous motion to reopen on October 26, 2012, which was properly denied on November 14, 2012. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that he relied to his detriment upon the statements of an individual in his attorney’s office. To the extent the respondent is alleging ineffective assistance by his attorney or a person in that office, he has not complied with the requirements to state a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Correa-Rivera v. Holder, 706 F.3d 1128, 1131 (9th Cir. 2013) (appeals asserting ineffective assistance claims are effectively motions to reopen); Al Ramahi v. Holder, 725 F.3d 1133, 1138-39 (9th Cir. 2013) (no error in failing to find ineffective assistance in the absence of evidentiary support required by Matter of Lozada).
 
In particular, the respondent is required to provide an affidavit describing in detail the agreement with counsel, inform counsel of the allegations and afford counsel an opportunity to respond, and report whether a complaint of ethical or legal violations has been filed with the proper authorities, and if not, why. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639. The respondent, however, has not satisfied any of these formal requirements. See Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999) (“[t]o establish ineffective assistance of counsel in a motion to reopen, an alien must” meet Lozada’s requirements). Nor do we discern any ineffective assistance plain on the face of the record. See Tamang v. Holder, 598 F.3d 1083, 1090-91 (9th Cir. 2010) (failure to satisfy Matter of Lozada requirements was fatal to ineffective assistance of counsel claim where ineffectiveness was not plain on the face of the record).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WALTER EDGARDO PORTILLO-PADILLA
File: AXXX XX9 744 - Atlanta, GA
February 27, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ninette M. Ponton, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jack D. Spencer
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent timely appeals an Immigration Judge’s August 11, 2017, decision denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of El Salvador. On September 1, 2005, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia. The respondent filed a timely motion to reopen, which the Immigration Judge denied on October 3, 2005. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent received proper notice, and that he had not demonstrated that his failure to appear was due to exceptional circumstances (IJ at 3, Oct. 3, 2005).
 
On July 20, 2017, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen, again alleging lack of notice. He also argued ineffective assistance of counsel and sought reopening sua sponte. The Immigration Judge denied his motion, and the respondent filed a timely Notice of Appeal. On September 19, 2017, the Board denied his motion for a stay, and the respondent was removed from the United States.
 
On appeal, the respondent indicated that he would file a brief on appeal, but he did not do so. In his Notice of Appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred 1) in denying his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel; 2) in denying reopening based on his meritorious applications for asylum and cancellation; and 3) in denying the motion without a hearing. The Department of Homeland Security has filed a brief seeking summary dismissal for failure to file a brief. Although the respondent cursorily preserved several issues in his Notice of Appeal, he has not meaningfully challenged these issues and has therefore waived them.1 See Lapaix v. Att’y Gen., 605 F.3d 1138, 1145 (11th Cir. 2010).
 
Even if the issues raised in the Notice of Appeal were preserved, the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s second, untimely filed motion to reopen. The motion to reopen was untimely filed and number-barred. Sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A) & (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). Contrary to his assertion in his Notice of Appeal, the respondent did not seek reopening for cancellation of removal, and did not attach a cancellation application to the motion or otherwise demonstrate prima facie eligibility for such relief. Moreover, there is no exception to the time and number bar for cancellation of removal.
 
*2 Although the respondent attached an asylum application to his motion, he did not demonstrate changed country conditions or circumstances so that an exception to the time and number limitation applies (IJ, Attachment to Order, August 11, 2017). Nor did he demonstrate prima facie eligibility for relief (Id.). INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 319 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 104-05 (1988); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992).
 
As to ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent did not demonstrate that he pursued his rights diligently over the intervening 11 years. Avila-Santoyo v. Atty Gen., 713 F.3d. 1357, 1363 n. 5 (11th Cir. 2013). See IJ, Attachment, August 11, 2017. In addition, the respondent did not comply with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Last, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny sua sponte reopening as the respondent did not show an exceptional situation justifying the exercise of the Immigration Judge’s sua sponte authority to reopen. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1). Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On appeal, the respondent did not preserve the question of lack of notice of his hearing. As such, this issue has been abandoned. Sepulveda v. Att’y Gen., 401 F.3d 1226, 1228 n. 2 (11th Cir.2005); Matter of L-G-H-, 26 I&N Dec. 365, 366 n.1 (BIA 2014).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EYEN YULISSA HILARIO
File: AXXX XX4 147 - New York, NY
February 26, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose A. Rojas, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision of September 28, 2017. In that decision, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s August 3, 2017, motion to reopen proceedings in which she was ordered removed in absentia on December 3, 2003. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a reply to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
A motion to reopen alleging exceptional circumstances for a failure to appear at a removal hearing must be filed within 180 days of the order of removal, and only one such motion is allowed. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3)(i) & 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen maintains that her failure to appear for her December 3, 2003, hearing was due to exceptional circumstances. The Immigration Judge noted that while the respondent’s hospitalization constituted exceptional circumstances for failing to appear for her hearing, the motion was untimely filed.
 
The respondent also alleges that her mother hired someone who she thought was an attorney to file a motion to continue the respondent’s hearing. A claim of “““ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an ‘exceptional circumstance’ warranting the reopening of a deportation order entered in absentia.” See generally Aris v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 595, 596 (2d Cir. 2008) (citation omitted). The respondent’s motion implies that she did not appear at her hearing because of ineffective assistance by an unknown person, but the motion is untimely, as it was filed well beyond 180 days after the removal order was entered.
 
As the Immigration Judge properly found, the respondent here has not demonstrated that she exercised due diligence in pursuing her claim. The respondent alleged in her statement supporting her motion to reopen that after failing to appear at her hearing, and after she was released from the hospital, she and her mother went to the attorney’s office and it was shut down. The respondent has not indicated that she took any measures to secure new counsel after discovering she had been apparently defrauded by the attorney, at which point the respondent should have discovered that the attorney her mother ostensibly hired had failed to file a motion to continue proceedings. Instead, the respondent waited to seek reopening until almost 14 years after entry of her removal order. Consequently, equitable tolling is not appropriate here. See Jian Hua Wang v. BIA, 508 F.3d 710, 715-16 (2d Cir. 2007) (providing that alien bears the burden of proving that she has exercised due diligence in the period between discovering the ineffectiveness of her representation and filing the motion).
 
*2 We find without merit the respondent’s appellate contention that she was not advised of the consequences of failure to appear at her hearing. The Notice to Appear (NTA), which the respondent received by personal service, contains advisals notifying of consequences of failing to appear, and she was provided oral notice in Spanish (Exh. 1).
 
We also agree with the Immigration Judge that no exceptional circumstances have been presented to warrant sua sponte reopening, and further did not establish that she exercised due diligence in filing her motion. Pursuant to our holding in Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), the power to reopen sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000) (burden is on the movant to show exceptional circumstances).
 
For the foregoing reasons, and those articulated by the Immigration Judge in her decision, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, pursuant to our authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e) (5).
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
David L Neal
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1897683 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.




2018 WL 1897698 (BIA)
 
Unpublished
  
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CLAUDIO MARTEZ-SANCHEZ A.K.A. CLAUDIO MARQUEZ SANCHEZ A.K.A. CLAUDIO MARQUEZ-SANCHEZ
File: AXXX XX6 914 - Atlanta, GA
February 22, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Massiel Silva, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s May 1, 2017, decision denying his October 25, 2016, motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) did not file a response to the appeal. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility determinations, under a clearly erroneous standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On July 27, 2016, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed to Mexico because he waived the opportunity to file his application for relief by not filing it by the deadline on March 11, 2013 (IJ at 2). See Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992); Matter of Jean, 17 I&N Dec. 100, 102 (BIA 1979); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (“If an application or document is not filed within the time set by the Immigration Judge, the opportunity to file that application shall be deemed waived.”). On October 25, 2016, the respondent filed a motion titled “Motion to Reopen Removal Proceedings Pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988)” (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen). On May 1, 2017, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent did not comply with the requirements to inform his prior counsel of the allegations leveled against him and to give him an opportunity to respond pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639, and he did not demonstrate that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s performance (IJ at 2-3).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in concluding that he did not meet the procedural requirements for proving an ineffective assistance of counsel claim (Respondent’s Br. at 8-9). Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639. The respondent also argues that his prior counsel’s performance prevented him timely filing his application for relief in Immigration Court and thereby prejudiced his claim for such relief (Respondent Br. at 9-10).
 
*2 We conclude that the respondent’s motion to reopen is supported by an affidavit attesting to relevant facts, evidence that the prior counsel was informed of the allegations and has been unresponsive, and a copy of the complaint filed with the Alabama State Bar (Motion to Reopen Tab C at 10-12, 17-19; Respondent’s Br. Tab C at 10-12). The respondent’s current counsel served the respondent’s prior counsel with a copy of the bar complaint (Motion to Reopen Tab C at 10-12, 17-19; Respondent’s Br. Tab C at 10-12). The respondent’s current counsel indicates that she and her staff requested the respondent’s file from his prior counsel several times between August 3, 2016 and September 9, 2016, but the requests were ignored (Motion to Reopen Tab C at 10-12). The record demonstrates that the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639.
 
We also conclude that that the respondent was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s failure to prepare and file his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. 1229b(b)(1), by the deadline set by the Immigration Judge in 2013. Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1273-74 (11th Cir. 2005) (an alien claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must also show prejudice); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 640. The respondent’s prior counsel represented the respondent for 3 years after the deadline for filing his application for relief expired. He did not file any motions or documents regarding the respondent’s case until he filed a motion to withdraw on July 26, 2016. The record indicates that the respondent is eligible to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act (Motion to Reopen Tab D at 45-48 and Tab E at 49). Recently, the respondent’s prior counsel has been unresponsive to inquiries regarding the respondent’s case; this supports the respondent’s claim that his prior counsel was previously unresponsive as well. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
*3 FUTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
Edward F. Kelly
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1897698 (BIA)

2018 WL 1897695 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VADIM IGOREVICH KARGUIN A.K.A. VADIM IGOREVICH KARGIN A.K.A. VADIM KARGIN
File: AXXX XX7 788 - New York, NY
February 22, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bryan C. Johnson-Xenitelis, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carol A. Moore
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative decision on June 30, 2017, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s written decision denying as a matter of discretion the respondent’s application for an inadmissibility waiver under former section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), and pretermitting his adjustment of status application. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen for further consideration of his section 212(h) inadmissibility waiver application based on an additional qualifying relative, given the recent birth of his United States citizen son. The respondent also seeks reopening to apply for asylum, withholding of removal, and protection under the United Nations Convention Against Torture based on his alleged sexual orientation. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
The birth of the respondent’s son, particularly considering his health issues, is an additional positive consideration concerning whether the respondent merits a section 212(h) waiver as a matter of discretion (Respondent’s Mot., tab D). We also recognize that the respondent’s continuing efforts to remain sober is a positive equity (Respondent’s Mot., tabs B, F-I). Nevertheless, the medical records do not indicate that his son’s head injury will have any long term adverse neurological implications (Respondent’s Mot., tab D at 35-36, 47, 89-90, 97-100). Nor do the records show that the child’s health concerns relating to his mother’s methadone use cannot be adequately managed (Respondent’s Mot., tab D). Overall, despite the respondent’s new equities and rehabilitation efforts, the adverse factors - particularly his extensive criminal history - still outweigh the positive considerations and we do not find an adequate basis for reopening. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992) (alien must satisfy “heavy burden” of establishing that if the proceedings were reopened the new evidence would likely change the result in the case); Poradisova v. Gonzales, 420 F.3d 70, 78 (2d Cir. 2005) (alien must establish a prima facie case for the underlying substantive relief sought in order to prevail on a motion to reopen).
 
Additionally, the respondent - for the first time - claims that he will be harmed in Russia because of his sexual orientation. But motions to reopen are not vehicles for presenting new claims that could have been raised at the hearing before the Immigration Judge. INS v. Wang, 450 U.S. 139, 141 (1981) (discussing motions to reopen); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (motions to reopen must be based on evidence that could not have been discovered or presented at the hearing before the Immigration Judge); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 2006). Similarly, we will not reopen the proceedings concerning the respondent’s assertion that the Immigration Judge may have granted his inadmissibility waiver application had she been aware of the respondent’s sexual orientation. While the respondent speculates that the Immigration Judge may have weighed the positive and negative equities differently had she been aware of the respondent’s current assertion that his sexual orientation led to his drug use relapses, he has not shown that he could not have reasonably raised this allegation before the Immigration Judge (Respondent’s Mot., tabs B at 5). Similarly, the respondent’s alleged shame concerning his bisexuality is insufficient to meet his burden of establishing that he could not have reasonably presented evidence of his sexual orientation at his hearing.
 
*2 Notwithstanding the respondent’s allegation that prior counsel deficiently represented him by not seeking relief based on the respondent’s sexual orientation, he has not meaningfully raised an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, as he has not shown compliance with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).
 
Nor has the respondent submitted sufficient evidence demonstrating a material change in Russia with respect to his claims. We have taken administrative notice of the Department of State’s 2015 and 2016 Country Reports on Human Rights Practices - Russia. Those reports, coupled with the evidence supporting the motion, reflect ongoing and substantially similar treatment of bisexuals since before the respondent’s July 2016 hearing (Respondent’s Mot., tab K).
 
Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1897695 (BIA)

    1  Case Reopened by IN RE: EDGAR VINICIO BARRAGAN, BIA, May 8, 2018
2018 WL 1897706 (BIA)
 
Unpublished
  
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDGAR VINICIO BARRAGAN
File: AXXX XX8 490 - New York, NY
February 21, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eric F. Lorenzo, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 7, 2017, denying his motion to reopen removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record reflects that the Notice to Appear (“NTA”) was mailed to the respondent on August 20, 2009, to his last known address at 158-26 78th Ave., Fresh Meadows, NY 11366. The NTA indicated that the respondent would be notified of his hearing date by the Immigration Court. The NTA also advised of the statutory address obligations associated with removal proceedings and of the consequences of failing to provide a current address (Exh. 1). On August 20, 2009, a notice was sent to the respondent at the address provided, advising him of his hearing on December 8, 2009, in New York, NY. The notice was not returned to the court as undeliverable. The respondent did not appear at the scheduled hearing and he was removed in absentia on December 9, 2009.
 
On August 14, 2017, the respondent, through counsel, filed a motion to reopen, claiming that medical illnesses and ineffective assistance of counsel constituted “extraordinary circumstances.” The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposed reopening. On September 7, 2017, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion for the following reasons: (1) for failure to identify the prior counsel who allegedly provided ineffective assistance of counsel and for not complying with any of the requirements for pursuing an ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639-40 (BIA 1988); (2) for not producing corroborating evidence of the alleged medical conditions; and (3) that despite the alleged medical issues, the respondent had opportunities to pursue his immigration case, but failed to do so.
 
On appeal, the respondent requests that we exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen his removal proceedings, alleging family hardship and seeking to apply for re-adjustment of status based on an approved marriage-based visa petition filed by his United States citizen wife (Respondent’s Br. at 7-9). We decline to exercise our discretion to reopen proceedings sua sponte because the respondent has not demonstrated exceptional circumstances. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (stating that “as a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”).
 
*2 The respondent previously alleged that his prior counsel provided ineffective assistance by failing to file a joint petition to remove the conditions on the respondent’s residency, which resulted in the issuance of the NTA and the respondent being placed into proceedings (Respondent’s Motion at 5). The respondent seems to have abandoned this argument on appeal, claiming that “several extraordinary circumstances took place which affected his ability to file his I-751 Petition,” none of which includes an ineffective assistance of counsel claim (Respondent’s Br. at 5).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that even accepting the respondent’s allegations of serious health and family problems, he had numerous periods in which to attend to his immigration situation. The respondent has not established that he made reasonable efforts to determine the status of his removal proceedings in light of the fact he was placed in proceedings in 2009, which led to the issuance of an in absentia order of removal that year. Further, the respondent was taken into immigration custody in 2013, as a result of the in absentia order, and he took no action to reopen his proceedings until 4 years later. He did pursue a family-based visa petition in 2014, but has not offered a persuasive explanation for why he did not file his motion sooner or take other action. Rather, the respondent acknowledges that in light of his problems, he “simply procrastinated” in pursuing his immigration case (Respondent’s Br. at Tab A).
 
While we acknowledge that the respondent and his family will undoubtedly be subject to hardship upon any separation, the respondent he has not established circumstances so exceptional as to trigger the limited authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. at 984 (holding that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship). In this regard, we point out that many aliens who have been present in the United States for a period of years have accumulated favorable equities subsequent to being ordered removed. See Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216, 1217-18 (BIA 2000) (explaining that the burden is on the movant to show exceptional circumstances). Accordingly, the request will be denied.
 
Insofar as the respondent asserts in his Notice of Appeal (“NOA”) that he did not receive notice of his hearing, this is not supported by the record. The record reflects that all the notices, including the in absentia order, were mailed to the respondent’s last known address of record and he continues to reside at the same address. Moreover, he has not submitted an affidavit or any other evidence to establish that either notice was not properly delivered to the last known address to rebut the presumption of proper delivery. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.26(c)-(d); Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665 (BIA 2008) (setting forth the standards for determining whether an alien has presented sufficient evidence to overcome the weaker presumption of delivery that attaches to notices sent by regular mail); Matter of C-R-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 677 (BIA 2008) (holding that the respondent overcame presumption of delivery by regular mail where he submitted affidavit stating he did not receive the notice, as well as evidence he had incentive to appear and exercised due diligence in promptly obtaining counsel and requesting reopening of the proceedings). The respondent therefore had notice of his immigration proceedings and we decline to reopen proceedings on this basis.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1897706 (BIA)

U.S. Department of Justice
