2018 WL 1871988 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIR MUSTAFA ALI
File: AXXX XX1 971 - Buffalo, NY
February 1, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Caroline L. Doss, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of India, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 6, 2017, denying his motion to reopen these removal proceedings. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on April 2, 2014.1 The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) did not file a response to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded only (1) upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (2) upon a motion to reopen filed at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act, or that the alien was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (ii) of the Act; see also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 722-23 (BIA 1999).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he did not receive notice of the hearing on April 2, 2014, because his first counsel did not inform him that he was required to appear (Respondent’s Br.). The respondent asserts that his first counsel’s submission of two defective motions demonstrates ineffective assistance without need of further inquiry (Id.). The respondent argues that his second counsel filed a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel but the motion was not in compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (Id.). The respondent also indicated that he intends to apply for a U Visa (Id.).
 
The respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear, advised of his address obligations, and the Notice of Hearing (NOH) was sent to the address he provided on the Form I-830, Notice to EOIR: Alien Address (IJ at 1; Exh. 1, NOH dated September 19, 2013). The respondent argues that he demonstrated a lack of notice or exceptional circumstances for not attending the scheduled hearing on April 2, 2014 (IJ at 1). 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Ineffective assistance of counsel may constitute such an exceptional circumstance if the ineffective assistance results in an alien’s failure to appear at his hearing. See Arts v. Mukasey, 517 F.3d 595, 599 (2d Cir. 2008). We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s motion to reopen based on his claim of ineffective assistance of two prior counsels does not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639-40 (BIA 1988). The respondent submitted an affidavit detailing his communications with his prior counsels. However, the respondent did not submit copies of letters sent to the two prior counsels whose performance he deemed deficient. Thus, the respondent’s prior counsels, whose integrity or competence is being impugned, were not given an opportunity to respond to the charges against them. Id. The respondent did not file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding the allegedly deficient representation or explain his failure to do so. See Ruiz-Martinez v. Mukasey, 516 F.3d 102, 121 (2d Cir. 2008) (recognizing that, while “slavish adherence” to Matter of Lozada is not required, when an alien does not comply with those requirements in any respect, the ineffective assistance of counsel claim is forfeited). We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not demonstrate that his first counsel prevented the respondent from appearing at the hearing held on April 2, 2014, or that the second counsel provided him ineffective assistance that prejudiced his claim (IJ at 1).
 
*2 We also deny the respondent’s request to reopen proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal based on his intent to pursue a U Visa application (Form I-918) with the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS). The respondent has not provided evidence that he submitted a U visa application to USCIS or that the application was received, such as by a Notice of Action (Form I-797). For the reasons stated above, the respondent has not demonstrated an appropriate basis to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on April 2, 2014.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On January 5, 2017, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s prior motion to reopen the in absentia order of removal entered on April 2, 2014, concluding the his failure to appear was due to his failure to notify the Immigration Court of his change of address. The Immigration Court mailed the Notice of Hearing to the address that the respondent provided and it was returned as not deliverable. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent was advised in the Notice to Appear and in the Form I-830, Notice to EOIR: Alien Address, to notify the Court of any change in his address but he did not do so. The respondent did not appeal.

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GERSHOM BWALYA MUBANGA JR.
File: AXXX XX6 550 - Baltimore, MD
January 30, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Oti Winifed Nwosu, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Zambia, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 10, 2017, which denied his motion to reopen removal proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not replied to the respondent’s brief on appeal. The record will be remanded.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2017). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record shows that in a July 12, 2011, order, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent a period of voluntary departure to November 9, 2011, with an alternate order of removal. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on July 5, 2017, based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel and seeking to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1) (2012). The DHS opposed the motion. On July 10, 2017, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen.
 
We conclude that a remand is warranted for the Immigration Judge to conduct fact-finding and legal analysis regarding the motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reopen cites only agreement with the DHS’s opposition; it does not include findings of fact or legal analysis to support the denial of the motion to reopen. See generally Matter of A-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 468, 473 (BIA 1999) (vesting the Immigration Judge with the responsibility for ensuring the “substantive completeness of the decision”). Therefore, we are unable to meaningfully review the Immigration Judge’s decision. Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2018 WL 1872002 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHEIKH SECK A.K.A. CHEZKH SECK
File: AXXX XX6 584 - El Paso, TX
January 30, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaime Jasso, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reinstatement of appeal
 
*1 The record before us shows that the respondent was ordered removed from the United States to Senegal on March 19, 2007. The Immigration Judge’s order reflects that the respondent waived his right to appeal the decision. On March 26, 2007, the respondent filed a pro se appeal. On April 2, 2007, the respondent filed a letter stating that he had changed his mind, wanted “[n]o more appeal,” wanted to go to Africa, and asked the Board to deny the appeal. The Board issued a decision on April 12, 2007, stating that the respondent had withdrawn his appeal and that the record was being returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
On December 6, 2017, the respondent filed a motion to reinstate his appeal. The motion argues that the respondent did not actually request withdrawal of appeal in his letter but asked that his appeal be denied and further that the letter indicates an incorrect belief that the respondent could challenge the Immigration Judge’s decision from Senegal. The respondent argues that he did not write the letter but that he found someone in the detention facility who advised him and who wrote the letter. The respondent’s motion further presents several arguments as to why he should not have been found subject to removal and that even if he were subject to removal, he qualified for relief that he had no opportunity to pursue.
 
Insofar as the respondent’s motion is a motion to reopen or a motion to reconsider for the purpose of reinstatement of the appeal, it is untimely. Motions to reconsider must be filed not later than 30 days after the date of the order from which reconsideration is sought. See section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(B); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Motions to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative order. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent filed his motion more than 10 years after the Board’s last decision. Moreover, the respondent does not argue that his motion falls within any statutory or regulatory exception to the filing requirements. Nor does the respondent claim that equitable tolling applies to his motion. See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337 (5th Cir. 2016).
 
Assuming the respondent is attempting to set out a case of ineffective assistance of counsel by the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge, he has not demonstrated compliance with the technical requirements for ineffective assistance claims as set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and (as noted previously), has not argued that equitable tolling applies.
 
*2 Finally, the respondent has not demonstrated that proceedings should be reopened or reconsidered sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The record shows that appeal was waived in this matter. Insofar as the respondent did appeal, he has not established that the Board incorrectly deemed his appeal withdrawn. The respondent’s letter to the Board in 2007 specifically said “No more appeal” and that the respondent wanted to go to Africa.
 
Moreover, more than 10 years have passed since the Board’s last order. The record does not show that the respondent has challenged that order until now. The respondent’s motion provides no information concerning what has taken place since the Board issued its order. We have no statements about where the respondent is living, whether he was removed to Senegal, or whether he attempted to find alternative counsel to assist him with his immigration issues. We conclude that the respondent has not sufficiently established an exceptional situation that would cause us to sua sponte reopen or reconsider and then reinstate the appeal. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (explaining that sua sponte reopening is reserved for exceptional situations).
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1872002 (BIA)
End of Document

2018 WL 1872017 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROGELIO GONZALEZ-HUERTA
File: AXXX XX6 849 - Chicago, IL
January 23, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Guy K. Weinstein, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michelle M. Venci
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on March 4, 2002, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. On August 1, 2017, the respondent filed another motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred. Sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000); Matter of L-V-K-, 22 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1999). In the motion, the respondent argues that the in absentia order of removal should be rescinded and his proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance of counsel of Attorney Abdullah Salah, who previously represented him until March 4, 2002. Specifically, the respondent argues that attorney Salah provided ineffective assistance by mistakenly advising him that his August 9, 2000, hearing was scheduled at 1 p.m. instead of 9 a.m. The respondent submitted a statement that, when he appeared at the Immigration Court at 1 p.m., he was informed that the hearing was scheduled earlier and he had been ordered removed in absentia.
 
We first note that, as stated in the Immigration Judge’s November 8, 2000, decision denying the respondent’s first motion to reopen, at a prior hearing the respondent was provided with oral notice of the hearing on August 9, 2000, as well as with written notice of hearing (IJ at 2). In any event, even assuming that respondent’s failure to appear at his August 9, 2000, hearing was due to the mistaken advice of attorney Salah, the respondent was last represented by this attorney in March 2002 when the Board denied his motion to reopen, and the respondent has not shown how he has acted with due diligence since that time during the period of more than 15 years before filing the current motion to reopen.
 
The respondent argues that the motion time limitations should be equitably tolled in his case because he relied on attorney Salah until March 2002 and furthermore he had retained another attorney in 2005 for consular processing of his visa (Motion, at 11). The United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit held that equitable tolling based on counsel’s performance requires, at a minimum, a showing that the alien exercised due diligence in seeking relief. Yusev v. Sessions, 851 F.3d 763, 767 (7th Cir. 2017); El-Gazawy v. Holder, 690 F.3d 852, 859 (7th Cir. 2012); Johnson v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 795, 799 (7th Cir. 2007); Patel v. Gonzales, 442 F.3d 1011, 1016 (7th Cir. 2006). The due diligence requirement is satisfied by a showing that the alien “““could not reasonably have been expected to file earlier.” Yusev v. Sessions, 851 F.3d at 767; Johnson v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d at 799; Pervaiz v. Gonzales, 405 F.3d 488, 490 (7th Cir. 2005); see also Patel v. Gonzales, 442 F.3d at 1016 (stating that equitable tolling requires a consideration of whether a reasonable person in the claimant’s position would have been aware of the possibility that he had suffered an injury).
 
*2 The respondent admits, and the record shows, that the respondent was aware of the in absentia order of removal in August 2000 (Motion, at 11; Motion Exh. B). Even accepting the respondent’s assertion that he relied on attorney Salah until March 2002, when the Board denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, the respondent has not shown why he could not reasonably have been expected to file earlier a motion claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. The fact that the respondent had retained a different attorney in 2005 for consular processing of his visa does not show that he could not reasonably have been expected to file an ineffective assistance of counsel motion to reopen earlier than August 2017. See also Galvez Pineda v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 833, 839 (10th Cir. 2005) (holding that removable aliens should not delay matters by pursuing multiple avenues of relief seriatim when no reason suggests why they could not be pursued simultaneously). The respondent’s general assertion in his statement that he “continued to investigate relief likewise does not establish due diligence during the period of more than 15 years (Motion Exh. B, at ¶ 20). Accordingly, the motion will be denied as untimely.
 
The respondent also urges us to sua sponte reopen his proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). We do not find that the respondent’s case presents an exceptional situation that would warrant the Board’s exercise of its discretion to reopen sua sponte. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the power to reopen sua sponte “is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”).
 
Based on the above, the respondent’s motion will be denied. The motion for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1872017 (BIA)

2018 WL 1872023 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AVAT SOSA-CACHO
File: AXXX XX3 168 - Cleveland, OH
January 22, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Amy M. Crossin, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; termination
 
*1 On September 20, 2013, we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s November 21, 2012, decision denying the respondent’s motion to administratively close his removal proceedings. The respondent has filed a motion to reopen to pursue adjustment of status based on an approved Form I-130 petition filed on his behalf by his United States citizen mother and a current priority date. The record before us does not contain a brief in opposition from the Department of Homeland Security. The motion to reopen and terminate will be denied.1
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely and will be denied as such. With certain exceptions not pertinent here, an alien is entitled to file one motion to reopen and the motion to reopen must be filed not later than 90 days after the final administrative order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent’s motion to reopen was filed on November 17, 2017, over 4 years after the final order of removal was entered, and the record before us does not present any exceptional situation warranting sua sponte reopening (see Motion to Reopen, Exhs. 1-25). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (stating that the power to sua sponte reopen proceedings is reserved for “““exceptional situations” and “is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship.”).
 
We acknowledge that the respondent has submitted numerous documents and statements in support of his motion, including documents regarding his ties to the United States and community, his character, and the potential hardship to his family, including his United States citizen children (see Motion to Reopen, Exhs. 2-15). However, we do not have the authority to grant relief from removal solely on equitable or humanitarian grounds. See Matter of Medina, 19 I&N Dec. 734, 742 (BIA 1988).
 
Finally, to the extent the respondent is alleging that his former counsel provided ineffective assistance (Motion to Reopen, Exh. 21), he has neither complied with the procedural requirements for such a claim nor submitted evidence to justify equitable tolling. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural and substantive requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim); Pepaj v. Mukasey, 509 F3d 725, 727 (6th Cir. 2007) (stating that an alien who does not comply with the Lozada requirements forfeits his ineffective assistance of counsel claim); Tapia-Martinez v. Gonzales, 482 F.3d 417, 422 (6th Cir. 2007) (stating that equitable tolling based on ineffective assistance of counsel requires a showing of due diligence). Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s untimely motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

On November 21, 2017, the Board denied the respondent’s request for a stay of removal pending adjudication of this motion.

2018 WL 1756894 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PETER NDUNGU CHEGE A.K.A. PETER NDUNGU
File: AXXX XX0 439 - Boston, MA
January 8, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Musa P. Sebadduka, Esquire
 
APPLICATIONS: Reopening; stay of removal
 
*1 This case was last before us on December 31, 2015, when we entered a final administrative order dismissing the respondent’s appeal. On July 14, 2017, the respondent submitted the instant motion to reopen pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. He requests a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the motion. The motion has been filed out of time, and it will be denied. The respondent’s stay request will also be denied.
 
With limited exceptions, a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In certain circumstances, an alien may toll the time limit applicable to his motion to reopen by demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In addition to complying with the procedural requirements for such a claim, the alien must show prejudice as a result of the ineffective assistance and that he exercised due diligence during the period he seeks to toll. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Kenya. He seeks reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent claims that when the Immigration Judge asked if he was going to request voluntary departure and his counsel said no, he asked his counsel what the Immigration Judge meant by voluntary departure, and his counsel replied that he would explain it after the hearing. See Exh. VIII, ¶ 10. He relates that his counsel later explained what voluntary departure meant, and stated that he did not need voluntary departure because the Immigration Judge erred and they were going to appeal his decision. Id., ¶ 11. He recounts that after his appeal was dismissed, his counsel advised him to seek review in the Circuit Court of Appeal, and said that if the Circuit Court remands his case to the Immigration Judge, he could apply for voluntary departure. Id., ¶ 13. The respondent states that he was confused and upset because his counsel was telling him that he needed to appeal one more time to achieve what the Immigration Judge had offered him at the start. Id. He reports that he agreed to pursue further appeals to the Circuit Court as a last alternative, and that on May 15, 2017, the Circuit Court affirmed the decision of the Board. Id., ¶ 15. The respondent claims that his counsel should not have appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision because he knew that he was inadmissible and barred from adjustment of status, and that he misrepresented the general outlook of his case, giving him false hope. Id., ¶¶ 16-17. He alleges that his counsel’s false representations cost him his only available option and several thousand dollars in legal fees and court costs that were not necessary, and violated the rules of professional conduct. Id., ¶¶ 18-20.
 
*2 The respondent offers his affidavit and his statement of facts, his notice to his former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance, his counsel’s response, the bar complaint he filed against his former counsel and receipt notice, a Massachusetts record of criminal offender information, and letters from a friend and a parish vicar in the United States.
 
The respondent has not offered persuasive evidence for his claim that his counsel made false representations to him. The record reflects that the respondent agreed to proceed with the appeal and federal court review of his removal proceedings after the Immigration Judge rendered his decision in 2014. We are not convinced by the respondent’s contention at this time, years later and after his appeal has been dismissed and petition for review denied, that his counsel provided ineffective assistance by pursuing those courses of action. We conclude that, although the respondent has substantially complied with the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not demonstrated that he suffered ineffective assistance. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639; Asaba v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 9, 12 (1st Cir. 2004); Saakian v. INS, 252 F.3d 21, 25 (1st Cir. 2001). We are not persuaded that equitable tolling of the time limit for motions to reopen is warranted in this case. See Bead v. Holder, 703 F.3d 591, 594 (1st Cir. 2013); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. at 553.
 
The motion to reopen has been filed out of time, and has not been shown to come within any exception to the timely filing requirements for motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent has not met his heavy burden to demonstrate that his removal proceedings should be reopened. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992). He has not shown the exceptional circumstances necessary to warrant our granting this untimely motion in the exercise of our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, as the motion exceeds the time limit for motions to reopen, it will be denied, and the respondent’s stay request will also be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen and stay request are denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2018 WL 1756894 (BIA)
End of Document

2018 WL 1756897 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JENNIFA JOSHUE A.K.A. JENNIFER JOSHUA
File: AXXX XX0 562 - Danbury, CT
January 4, 2018
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul B. Grotas, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on January 9, 2009, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely filed. On July 11, 2017, the instant untimely, number-barred motion to reopen was filed by the respondent. The motion will be denied.
 
Under the law, with certain exceptions not pertinent here, a party is limited to one motion to reopen. Section 240(c)(7)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). As this is the respondent’s second motion to reopen, it is numerically barred. The motion to reopen has also been filed more than 90 days after our decision dismissing the appeal on January 21, 2003, that resulted in a final administrative order of removal. Accordingly, as with the respondent’s previous motion, the instant motion to reopen is untimely.
 
We do not find that there are exceptional circumstances in this case that warrant reopening under our limited sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). See also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).1 Moreover, an alien’s potential eligibility for relief after the entry of a final administrative removal order is not, in and of itself, an exceptional circumstance. This is a common situation for many aliens. The respondent asserts, however, that because she was the victim of “fraud and deceit” by someone who was not an attorney, she has significant equities, and her removal would be an extreme hardship on her United States citizen husband and children, her proceedings should be sua sponte reopened (Respondent’s Motion at 14-15).
 
The respondent alleges that she paid and worked closely with an individual, Mr. Victor Espinal, who was not, in fact, an attorney. Mr. Espinal was convicted in 2009, of fraudulently representing himself as an attorney (Respondent’s Motion at 7). Despite the seriousness of these circumstances, the record indicates that Mr. Espinal did not represent the respondent in her removal proceedings; Mr. Espinal never filed a notice of appearance, Form EOIR-28, before the Immigration Court or a Form EOIR-27 before the Board on behalf of the respondent (Respondent’s Motion at 6; Exhs. D, E, M, P).2 Given the lack of representation by Mr. Espinal, we find no basis for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against him.
 
With regard to the allegations made against Mr. Rojas, the respondent has not sufficiently met all of the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F. 2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. We find the respondent’s affidavit to be inadequate as it fails to provide adequate details about the nature of the agreement between the respondent and Mr. Rojas, and the agreed upon scope of their representation. See Piranej v. Mukasey, 516 F.3d 137 (2d Cir. 2008) (remanding for further fact-finding in the context of a general retainer agreement). We have considered the other evidence submitted by the respondent, including the payment receipts and correspondence, but conclude that the respondent has not met the requirements of Lozada. Moreover, the respondent has not shown prejudice as a result of any alleged ineffective assistance, as she has not shown that the outcome of her case would likely have been different but for the claimed ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009).
 
*2 We turn next to the respondent’s contention that the Second Circuit’s decision in Husic v. Holder, 776 F.3d 59 (2d Cir. 2015), constitutes a fundamental change in the law which renders her eligible for a discretionary waiver under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h) (Respondent’s Motion at 12). We acknowledge the change in the law. See also Matter of J-H-J, 26 I&N Dec. 563 (BIA 2015) (holding that section 212(h) of the Act only precludes aliens who entered the United States as lawful permanent residents from establishing eligibility for a waiver on the basis of an aggravated felony conviction).
 
Notwithstanding the change in law, the respondent is not currently the beneficiary of an approved Form I-130 (Petition for Alien Relative), so as to be prima facie eligible for adjustment of status, even if granted a section 212(h) waiver. In addition, “given the evidence of record regarding the respondent’s conviction under counts 1 and 5 of the relevant superseding indictment (Exh. 3),” we remain unconvinced that the waiver (or adjustment of status) would or should be granted in the exercise of discretion. See Decision of the Board of Immigration Appeals dated January 9, 2009.
 
For all of the foregoing reasons, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that although the respondent in a footnote briefly references a question whether her conviction was for an aggravated felony, she has not meaningfully raised or argued the issue.

2

Mr. Espinal never represented the respondent in her claims before the U.S. District Court for the Eastern District of New York (Exh. H), or before the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit (Exh. J).


2018 WL 1756897 (BIA)

2017 WL 8787194 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JINGSHAN LI
File: AXXX XX4 679 - San Diego, CA
December 29, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Chunping Shen, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reissuance
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on July 23, 2002, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision. On October 23, 2017, more than 15 years after the Board’s order, the respondent filed a motion to reissue the Board’s July 23, 2002, decision. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
In the motion, the respondent argues that the Board’s prior decision should be reissued based on the ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney David Z. Su, who represented him in his petition for review filed before the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit.1 Specifically, the respondent argues that attorney Su failed to file an opening brief before the Ninth Circuit, resulting in the dismissal of his petition for review by the Ninth Circuit on or about November 26, 2003 (Motion, at 2). The respondent further asserts that attorney Su did not inform him about the Ninth Circuit’s dismissal of his petition for review and he only learned about it when he received a July 17, 2017, Notice of Administrative Closure from the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (Motion Exh. 4).
 
The Board has on occasion reissued its decisions, but generally only due to Board error or administrative problems involving the service of the Board’s decisions. The respondent admits, and the record indicates, that the Board’s prior decision was served on the counsel of record at the time, and the respondent in fact was aware of the Board’s decision and filed a timely petition for review through attorney Su (Motion Exh. 1). Since there was no Board error or administrative problems involving the service of the Board’s prior decision, reissuance of the decision is not warranted.
 
Furthermore, reissuance of the Board’s prior decision is not warranted based on the ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Su. In Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), the Attorney General concluded that the Board has the discretion to consider claims of ineffective assistance of counsel based on conduct of the counsel that occurred after a final order of removal had been entered. The scope of such discretion was left for the Board to determine. Id. Inasmuch as the claimed ineffective assistance of counsel affected the respondent’s case before the Ninth Circuit, a different tribunal in a different branch of the Government, we decline to consider the ineffective assistance of counsel against attorney Su.2
 
We also decline to sua sponte reopen, reconsider, or reissue in the exercise of discretion under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) based on the totality of circumstances presented. Based on the above, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reissue is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent was represented by a different attorney at his hearing before the Immigration Judge and on appeal before the Board.

2

While this would not affect the outcome of the respondent’s motion, we also note that the respondent’s motion includes limited evidence of his due diligence in pursuing the ineffective assistance of counsel claim during the period of more than 14 years since the Ninth Circuit dismissed his petition for review. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that held that the 90-day motion to reopen time limitations may be equitably tolled based on ineffective assistance of counsel when the alien was “prevented from filing [a motion to reopen] because of deception, fraud, or error, as long as the [alien] acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error” by the attorney); see also Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672 (9th Cir. 2011). The respondent’s declaration states that attorney Su “never told me the final result of the appeal to [the Ninth] Circuit but kept telling me the case was still pending” (Motion Exh. 1, at ¶ 8). However, the respondent does not specify when was the last time he inquired with attorney Su about his petition for review.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS ALBERTO LOPEZ
File: AXXX XX3 311 - New York, NY
December 22, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William Lopez-Ruiz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on September 22, 2017, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision finding his application for adjustment of status abandoned and alternatively finding the respondent statutorily ineligible for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen urging that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. He also requests a stay of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded to the motion. The motion and stay will be denied.
 
Applicable regulations provide that a motion to reopen “shall state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Moreover, a movant must satisfy the “heavy burden” of establishing that if the proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). To prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, an alien must show that competent counsel would have acted otherwise and that the alien was prejudiced. Romero v. INS, 399 F.3d 109, 112 (2d Cir. 2005); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent urges that the attorney who represented him at his hearing below was ineffective for failing to timely file his application for adjustment of status, which resulted in it being deemed abandoned. However, the Immigration Judge and the Board alternatively denied the application on its merits. Specifically, we found that the respondent did not establish the requisite parent child relationship for purposes of adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act as a derivative grandfathered alien. The respondent has not submitted any new or additional evidence to establish that he once qualified as his father’s child as defined under section 101(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(b)(1). Therefore, the respondent still has not established eligibility for adjustment of status. As such, he has not demonstrated prejudice from counsel’s actions or omissions such that he has established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has not submitted persuasive evidence that is sufficient to meet his “heavy burden” of showing that it is likely that the result would change if the proceedings were reopened. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. at 464. Therefore, we do not find that he has met his burden of showing that reopening of these proceedings is warranted.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
David L Neal
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: LEANDRO SOARES LACERDA
File: AXXX XX6 785 - San Diego, CA
December 13, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anne Doebler, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Immigration Judge’s June 12, 2002, written decision became the final administrative order when the Board summarily affirmed it in July 2003. The Immigration Judge’s decision denied a previous motion to reopen these proceedings in which the respondent was ordered removed in absentia on January 15, 2002, under section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), as the respondent did not attend his hearing despite being served with the notice of hearing at the address he provided the Immigration Court. More than 15 years later the respondent again seeks reopening, averring that prior counsel deficiently represented him by advising him not to attend his hearing, because counsel erroneously believed it had been postponed. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent reiterates the same argument presented in his February 21, 2002, motion before the Immigration Judge (Respondent’s Mot. to Reopen, February 21, 2002). We will not revisit the Immigration Judge’s finding that this allegation - coupled with the other evidence supporting the previous motion - did not constitute exceptional circumstances to warrant rescission of his in absentia removal order, as a motion to reopen is not an opportunity to repeat previously-considered arguments. See INS v. Wang, 450 U.S. 139, 141 (1981) (discussing motions to reopen); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991); see generally sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (e)(1) of the Act.
 
Nor has the respondent demonstrated the requisite due diligence to raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (observing that equitable tolling is predicated on alien identifying and pursuing an ineffective assistance claim with due diligence); Bataev v. Mukasey, 276 F. App’x. 581 (9th Cir. 2008) (affirming Board’s finding that alien did not act with due diligence, where he waited over 1 year to raise ineffective assistance of counsel claim). At the very latest, the respondent was aware of prior counsel’s alleged deficient performance when the Immigration Judge issued his June 12, 2002, decision denying the motion. It was incumbent upon him to diligently pursue an ineffective assistance of counsel claim then, rather than waiting over 15 years to file the current motion.
 
Finally, the respondent has not established an exceptional situation warranting sua sponte reopening. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant

2017 WL 8787246 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARCIO JUNIOR CUCCOLO A.K.A. MARCIO JUNIOR BATISTA-IZAIAS A.K.A. MARCIO B. IZAIAS
File: AXXX XX5 684 - Newark, NJ
December 11, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mikhail Usher, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 This case is before us pursuant to a February 24, 2016, order of the United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit granting the government’s unopposed motion to remand the record for further consideration. The appeal will be dismissed.1
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2017). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The government requested a remand to allow the Board to consider the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s continuance request and any other issues that we deemed appropriate. There have been many significant developments in the law surrounding the application of the categorical and modified categorical approach and the determination of whether or not a statute is divisible for purposes of determining whether a conviction is an aggravated felony. Therefore, we will address this issue in addition to the denial of the continuance.
 
We find the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent any further continuance in this case. Further, we conclude that the criminal statute at issue is divisible under current case law, and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision that the respondent’s conviction for possessing, distributing, manufacturing, dispensing a controlled dangerous drug (Oxycodone), in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5(a)(1) (2013) is an aggravated felony (IJ at 2-3). Section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B).
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit, in whose jurisdiction these proceedings arise, has identified two “routes” to determine when a state drug conviction constitutes an aggravated felony: the “illicit trafficking” route and the “hypothetical felony” route, both tracking the statutory language. Gerbier v. Holmes, 280 F.3d 297, 305 (3d Cir. 2002) (citing Matter of Davis, 20 I&N Dec. 536 (BIA 1992)). Although we previously found otherwise, under more recent case law, we now conclude that the respondent has not been convicted of an aggravated felony pursuant to the “““illicit trafficking” route,” because under New Jersey law, “distribution” and ““dispensing” include no remuneration requirement. See Chang-Cruz v. Attorney General of the United States, 659 Fed. Appx. 114 (3d Cir. Aug. 24, 2016) (unpublished) (citing N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-2) and Matter of G.R., 929 A.2d 602, 605 (2007)). The conviction continues to be an aggravated felony, however, under the “hypothetical felony” route.
 
*2 Under the “hypothetical felony” route, an aggravated felony is defined in pertinent part as the “illicit trafficking in a controlled substance (as defined in section 102 of the Controlled Substances Act [21 U.S.C. § 802]), including a drug trafficking crime (as defined in section 924(c) of title 18, United States Code.” Section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act (emphasis added). 18 U.S.C. § 924(c)(2) defines a “drug trafficking crime” as “any felony punishable under the Controlled Substances Act” (CSA). The United States Supreme Court has held that an offense (whether labeled a misdemeanor or a felony by the relevant jurisdiction) constitutes a drug trafficking crime if it “proscribes conduct punishable as a felony” under the CSA. Moncrieffe v. Holder, 133 S.Ct. 1678, 1683 (2013) (quoting Lopez v. Gonzales, 549 U.S. 47, 60 (2006)). Thus, an offense comes within the “hypothetical felony” route when it necessarily prohibits conduct that the CSA likewise prohibits and punishes it by more than 1 year of imprisonment. Id. at 1683 (citing 18 U.S.C. § 3559(a)(5)).
 
In determining whether a state offense is a hypothetical federal felony, we apply the “categorical approach,” where we “focus solely on whether the elements of the crime of conviction sufficiently match the elements of [the] generic [federal offense], while ignoring the particular facts of the case.” Mathis v. United States, 136 S. Ct. 2243, 2248 (2016). This entails looking to whether the elements of the state statute of conviction categorically fit within the “generic” federal crime. Id. It is required to consider only the respective elements of the state and federal offenses to see whether a conviction under the state statute “‘necessarily’ involved facts equating to the generic federal offense.” Moncrieffe v. Holder, 133 S.Ct. at 1684 (quoting Shepard v. United States, 544 U.S. 13, 24 (2005) (plurality opinion). We do not look to the facts of the particular case, but rather look to see “whether the state statute defining the crime of conviction categorically fits within the generic federal definition of a corresponding aggravated felony.” See Singh v. Attorney General of the United States, 839 F.3d 273, 278 (3d Cir. 2016) (citing Moncrieffe v. Holder, 133 S.Ct. at 1684). Thus, under the categorical approach, the respondent’s state drug conviction is an aggravated felony if the conduct necessary to sustain a state conviction includes all of the elements of an offense that could be convicted as a felony under the CSA (21 U.S.C. § 801 et seq.). See Gerbier v. Holmes, 280 F.3d at 803.
 
*3 The Immigration Judge may depart from the categorical approach to determine whether the statute of conviction is an aggravated felony if the statute is divisible. A statute is divisible if (a) it lists multiple discrete offenses as enumerated alternatives or defines a single offense by reference to disjunctive sets of “elements,” more than one combination of which could support a conviction; and (b) some (but not all) of those listed offenses or combinations of disjunctive elements are a categorical match to the relevant generic standard. Descamps v. United States, 133 S.Ct. 2276, 2283 (2013); see also United States v. Brown, 765 F.3d 278, 295 (3d Cir. 2014). Further, any facts that increase the prescribed range of penalties to which a criminal defendant is exposed are elements of the crime, and must be found beyond a reasonable doubt. United States v. Abbott, 748 F.3d 154, 159 (3d Cir. 2014) (citing Alleyne v. United States, 133 S.Ct. 2151, 2160 (2013) (quoting Apprendi v. New Jersey, 530 U.S. 466, 490 (2000)). In a case in which the type of controlled substance is not specified in the statute of conviction, but the type of controlled substance impacts the sentencing determination, the type of controlled substance is an element of the offense. Id. at 158-59.
 
At the time the respondent committed the offense, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5(a)(1) provided that it shall be unlawful for any person knowingly or purposely to manufacture, distribute or dispense, or to possess or have under his control with intent to manufacture, distribute or dispense, a controlled dangerous substance or controlled substance analog. The analogous federal statute under the CSA is 21 U.S.C. § 841(a)(1). Wilson v. Ashcroft, 350 F.3d 377, 381 (3d Cir. 2003).
 
Although the respondent argues that the statute is not divisible under Descamps, we disagree. The statute here is divisible because the type of controlled substance involved must be proven beyond a reasonable doubt by a jury. The jury instructions for N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5(a)(1) make it clear that the jury must find (a) the conduct involved; (b) the type of controlled substance involved; and (c) that the individual acted knowingly and purposefully. See N.J. J.I. Crim. § 2C:35-5. Given that more than one combination of elements could support a conviction and can impact the range of penalties, the statute is divisible, warranting application of the modified categorical approach. See Evanson v. Attorney General of U.S., 550 F.3d 284, 293 (3d Cir. 2008).
 
*4 A review of the documents in the respondent’s record of conviction establishes that the respondent pled guilty to indictment number 13-08-01039-A (Exh. 2). This indictment and the criminal accusation charge the respondent with possessing, distributing, manufacturing, dispensing a controlled dangerous drug (Oxycodone), in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-5(a)(1) (Exhs. 2A and 2B). Oxycodone is a Schedule II controlled substance, 21 U.S.C. § 812(c); 21 C.F.R. § 1308.12(b)(1)(xiii), and the distribution of it is punishable by a term of imprisonment of more than 1 year. See 21 U.S.C. § 841(b). As the respondent’s offense of conviction is analogous to 21 U.S.C. § 841(a)(1), the respondent has been convicted of an aggravated felony for purposes of removability.2
 
We next consider the Immigration Judge’s denial of a further continuance. The respondent argues that he should have been provided more time to retain counsel with experience in both immigration and criminal law, in part, because he was pursuing post-conviction relief (Respondent’s Br. at 14-16, citing Padilla v. Kentucky, 559 U.S. 356 (2010)).3 He also argues that he was misinformed by two previous attorneys, but he has made no effort to substantially comply with the procedural requirements to establish ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Fadiga v. Attorney General of the U.S., 488 F.3d 142, 155 (3d Cir. 2007); Matter of Compean, Bangley & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). As such, there is no basis to remand the record as the respondent requests (Respondent’s Br. at 16).
 
The respondent was served with his Notice to Appear on December 1, 2014, and first appeared before an Immigration Judge on December 24, 2014 (Tr. at 1; Exh. 1). He was given a continuance to obtain representation (Tr. at 2). One month later, on January 22, 2015, he appeared without an attorney and was granted a continuance, but was informed that he would be required to go forward without an attorney should he not have one at the next hearing (Tr. at 4). On March 10, 2015, he again requested more time to obtain representation (Tr. at 6-12). The respondent was granted additional time to prepare his application for asylum and again appeared without an attorney on March 26, 2015 (Tr. at 17-18). At this hearing, he first indicated he no longer wished to apply for asylum, but changed his mind when he learned there was no other relief from removal available to him (Tr. at 20-23). He was then granted additional time to prepare his application, but at the final hearing, on April 16, 2015, again without representation, he stated that he would not be applying for asylum (Tr. at 29). Subsequently, the Immigration Judge entered his decision.
 
*5 The decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge and will not be overturned on appeal to the Board unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29; Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433, 434 (BIA 1987); see also Melgar v. Attorney Gen. of U.S., 442 Fed. App’x 695, 699 (3d Cir. 2011). Here, the respondent was provided ample time to obtain representation and the Immigration Judge properly denied the continuance. Also, as discussed above, the respondent has not shown he is eligible for any relief from removal. The respondent has not demonstrated that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case or that substantial prejudice resulted. See Fadiga v. Attorney General of the U.S., 488 F.3d at 155; Khan v. Attorney General, 448 F.3d 226, 236 (3d Cir. 2006).
 
Based on the foregoing, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD

Matter of Gabryelsky, 20 I&N Dec. 750 (BIA 1993); see generally Matter of Khalik, 17 I&N Dec. 518 (BIA 1980). We further observe that the Immigration Judge’s order was entered on April 16, 2015, and there is no indication in the record that any challenge to his conviction has been successful.

2017 WL 8785840 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FAFA TOURAY
File: AXXX XX9 511 - Seattle, WA
December 8, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stephen C. Brown, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Margaret LaDow
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s June 13, 2017, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order entered on February 6, 2016. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i)(2017). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in declining to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i). The basis of the motion, which was filed on May 4, 2017, is that the respondent’s prior counsel neglected to remind him of the date and time of the February 6, 2016, hearing. The respondent argues that this amounts to ineffective assistance of counsel and thus “exceptional circumstances” excusing his absence from the February 6, 2016, hearing.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not substantially complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim (IJ at 3-4). See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 597 (9th Cir. 2004) (discussing the circumstances under which compliance with these procedural requirements may be excused). In particular, the respondent’s supporting documents do not provide sufficient detail of the agreement with prior counsel, including the extent of prior counsel’s representation. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639 (providing that a party claiming ineffective assistance of counsel should provide “an affidavit . . . that sets forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken . . . and what counsel did or did not represent to the respondent in this regard”). While the supporting affidavit and text messages clearly reflect that the respondent entered into an agreement with prior counsel concerning the underlying visa petition, it is unclear if the agreement encompassed representation in these removal proceedings (IJ at 3; cf. Aff. at ¶8).1 That prior counsel retained copies of the respondent’s immigration records, including a copy of the underlying hearing notice, does not persuade us of an agreement by prior counsel to represent the respondent in these removal proceedings (cf. Respondent’s Br. at 10-11). For these reasons, we conclude that the respondent has not properly raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.2
 
*2 Even assuming the respondent has properly raised this claim, we further agree with the Immigration Judge that the motion is untimely (IJ at 2). A motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal on the basis of exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days of the date of entry the order. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. While this period may be equitably tolled as the result of ineffective assistance of counsel, the tolling period is not indefinite. See Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d 672, 679 (9th Cir. 2011). Rather, once an alien “definitively learns of the harm” resulting from the complained instance of deficient performance or “obtains “‘vital information bearing on the existence of his claim”DD’, the tolling period ends and the statutory deadline begins to run. Id.; Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
The Immigration Judge concluded that the tolling period ended on February 15, 2016 (IJ at 2). Contrary to the respondent’s assertions on appeal (Respondent’s Br. at 5-6), we agree with the Immigration Judge that any tolling ended on this date. As noted in the affidavit accompanying the motion, this is the date that the respondent was advised by prior counsel that he had missed the hearing and would need to file a motion to reopen (IJ at 2; Aff. at ¶11). The respondent “““definitively learn[ed] of the harm” resulting from the complained instance of deficient performance--prior counsel’s failure to remind him of the hearing date--upon receiving this information (IJ at 2). Avagyan v. Holder, 646 F.3d at 679. Indeed, as the respondent concedes on appeal, upon receiving this information from prior counsel, the “[r]espondent became aware of potential grave consequences of missing his hearing” (Respondent’s Br. at 6). Thus, consultation with current counsel and current counsel’s review of the respondent’s file was not necessary to definitively learn of the harm caused by the alleged misconduct of prior counsel (see id.). Cf. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d at 895.
 
As the Immigration Judge properly determined, with the tolling period ending on February 15, 2016, the 180-day deadline extended to August 15, 2016 (IJ at 2). The May 4, 2017, motion is therefore untimely.
 
We further agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated that the circumstances surrounding his failure to appear amount to “exceptional circumstances” within the meaning of the Act. “The term “‘exceptional circumstances’ refers to exceptional circumstances (such as battery or extreme cruelty to the alien or any child or parent of the alien, serious illness of the alien, or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances) beyond the control of the alien.” Section 240(e)(1) of the Act (emphasis added); see also Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503, 509 (BIA 1996). The “particularized facts presented in each case” are relevant to determining whether the respondent has established “exceptional circumstances,” and the “plain language of the statute indicates that this is a difficult burden to meet.” Arredondo v. Lynch, 824 F.3d 801, 806 (9th Cir. 2016) (internal quotation marks and citations omitted).
 
*3 As an initial matter, we emphasize that this is not a case where the respondent was misinformed by his attorney, either that there was no hearing or that the hearing was on an incorrect date. Cf Monjaraz-Munoz v. INS, 327 F.3d 892, 896 (9th Cir. 2003), as amended, 339 F.3d 1012 (9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472, 474 (BIA 1996). Nor is this a case where the respondent was uninformed of a hearing date after notice was provided to his attorney. To the contrary, the respondent admits receiving the Notice of Hearing in Removal Proceedings advising him of the February 6, 2016, hearing before the Seattle Immigration Court (Exh. 2; Aff. at ¶¶7-8). The correct hearing date and time were therefore conveyed to the respondent, and this information was not contradicted by any actions of prior counsel.
 
While we recognize that the respondent eventually gave the original copy of the hearing notice to prior counsel (Aff. at ¶¶7-10), the respondent’s later confusion about the hearing date and his own lack of record keeping were not beyond his control (IJ at 4). Furthermore, despite the respondent’s claim that prior counsel failed to respond to his inquiries regarding the hearing status, the respondent always had the option, which he never exercised, of contacting the Immigration Court to learn the details of this hearing (Id. at 3).3 Considering the record in its entirety, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not demonstrated exceptional circumstances within the meaning of section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act.
 
For these reasons, we conclude that the Immigration Judge properly concluded that the respondent has not demonstrated that reopening is warranted under section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act. See INS v. Bagamasbad, 429 U.S. 24, 25-26 (1976) (as a general rule, courts and agencies are not required to make findings on issues the decision of which is unnecessary to the results they reach).
 
In conclusion, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to not exercise his sua sponte reopening authority in this case (IJ at 4). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent potentially becoming eligible for additional relief after the period for timely reopening expired does establish exceptional circumstances so as to provide a basis for granting reopening sua sponte (cf. Respondent’s Br. at 12-13). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

There is no record of prior counsel entering an appearance in these proceedings.

2

We do not share in the Immigration Judge’s concerns regarding the lack of notice to prior counsel and the lack of a complaint to appropriate disciplinary authorities (IJ at 3-4), in light of the evidence presented that prior counsel has been suspended from the practice of law (Mot. at 58). See Morales Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893, 896-97 (9th Cir. 2008) (excusing compliance with these requirements as futile under similar circumstances).

3

Additionally, while we recognize the respondent’s contention that he did not know his alien identification number (A-number) (Aff. at ¶10), there is is a considerable difference between being denied access to information for not providing an A-number and not making any attempt to contact the Immigration Court.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PABLO AGUILAR-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX0 019 - San Diego, CA
December 1, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James B. Rudolph, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Amelia C. Anderson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 29, 2014, which denied his motion to reopen removal proceedings. Pursuant to the Board’s October 11, 2016, remand, the Immigration Judge issued another decision on June 22, 2017,1 further specifying the reasons for denying the motion to reopen. Thereafter, the parties provided arguments on appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2017). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The record shows that the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) placed the respondent in removal proceedings with personal service of the Notice to Appear on August 12, 2013, alleging that he was a native and citizen of Mexico and charging him as inadmissible under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i) (2012) (present without being admitted or paroled) (Exh. 1). The record indicates that the respondent, who was represented by counsel, admitted the allegations and conceded the charge. On May 28, 2014, the respondent applied solely for pre-conclusion voluntary departure, which the Immigration Judge granted with an alternate order of removal to Mexico.
 
The respondent, through new counsel, filed a motion to reopen on September 30, 2014, alleging ineffective assistance from former counsel. The DHS opposed the motion. In the October 29, 2014, decision, the Immigration Judge adopted the reasoning from the DHS opposition and denied the motion to reopen without further reasoning.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s June 22, 2017, denial of the motion to reopen. The motion is untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent concedes that the motion is untimely but contends that the 90-day filing requirement should be equitably tolled on account of ineffective assistance from former counsel. However, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), because he has not filed a complaint concerning former counsel with the State bar or reasonably explained why.
 
*2 In this regard, the respondent argues that filing a bar complaint is not required because former counsel has been substantially compliant because he has responded to the letter from current counsel and because he provided a reason for not filing a motion to suppress. Respondent’s Br. at 6-7. However, in the context of an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, “substantial compliance” with the Lozada requirements generally refers to substantial compliance by the complaining party, the respondent, not the attorney whose competence is being challenged.
 
In addition, to date, the respondent has not substantiated at all the allegation that he was the subject of racial or ethnic profiling by local police, which is the underlying basis for the ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Thus, there is no sufficient basis to absolve the respondent from filing a State bar complaint against his former attorney or, by extension, to equitably toll the filing deadline for the motion to reopen.
 
We note that the respondent states in the brief on appeal that former counsel was suspended from the practice of law on November 13, 2014, and on December 21, 2015, and was disbarred on August 14, 2016. Respondent’s Br. at 7. However, those disciplinary actions do not relieve the respondent of complying with the bar complaint requirement when he filed his motion to reopen on September 30, 2014. See Morales Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893, 897 (9th Cir. 2009).
 
In conclusion, we agree that the motion to reopen is untimely and that the respondent has not shown that the 90-day filing requirement should be equitably tolled. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

1

We note that the decision is dated June 22, 2016, which appears to be an administrative or clerical error. The decision was served by mail on the parties on June 23, 2017.

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION

Lorena Soto Rivas-Tiemann, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s June 5, 2017, decision ordering him removed to Mexico. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The Immigration Judge granted the respondent a continuance on May 8, 2017, so he could have an opportunity to retain counsel. The respondent was not able to retain counsel. On May 18, 2017, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and, as the respondent did not appear eligible for any form of relief, he ordered the respondent removed (IJ at 2). On appeal the respondent, now represented by counsel, alleges ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, he argues that his actions before the Immigration Judge, when he appeared pro se, constitute ineffective assistance of counsel as he did not request a continuance to renew his DACA status (Respondent’s Br. at 5-8).
 
The respondent cannot claim ineffective assistance by counsel due to his own conduct while he proceeded pro se. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is removable as charged and that there is no relief available to him.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Hugh G. Mullane

2017 WL 8785875 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MELVIN ANTONIO DIAZ-CASTRO
File: AXXX XX0 462 - Houston, TX
November 28, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Randy Alexander, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on November 30, 2016, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s January 27, 2016, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind the order of removal entered in absentia on May 2, 2006. On April 24, 2017, the respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador filed the present request for reopening. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent does not dispute that the present motion exceeds the time limits applicable to motions to reopen. Rather, he contends that such limits should be equitably tolled because he received ineffective assistance from former counsel. The respondent asserts that, but for former counsel’s deficient performance, he would have succeeded on his asserted lack of proper notice claim on appeal before the Board.
 
However, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements for advancing a claim against former counsel, including the requirement that he show prejudice resulting from former counsel’s alleged deficient performance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 n.2 (5th Cir. 2001) (noting that to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate that counsel’s performance resulted in substantial prejudice); Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F. 3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994) (proving prejudice requires the petitioner to make a prima facie showing that, absent counsel’s deficient performance, he would have been entitled to the relief the sought).
 
It is undisputed that the respondent was personally served with the Notice to Appear, which included the date, time, and place of his scheduled hearing and was read to him in Spanish. As the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent’s claimed inability to understand that he was required to appear in court as directed does not defeat the fact that the respondent was personally served with and received proper notice of the date, time, and place of his May 2006 scheduled hearing (I.J. at 2), which was not reversed by the Board.
 
Moreover, the respondent has proffered no argument or evidence addressing the full breadth of both the Immigration Judge’s and the Board’s reasoning, namely that the respondent did not exercise due diligence in waiting more than 9 years to file a motion to reopen and rescind the May 2006 in absentia removal order. Thus, the respondent has not demonstrated that, absent counsel’s alleged deficient performance, he would have succeeded on appeal before the Board. See Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F. 3d at 85.
 
*2 Moreover, pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 212.7(e)(4)(iv), the respondent does not need to have his removal proceedings reopened to be able to apply for a provisional unlawful presence waiver with the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (“USCIS”). We also note that the respondent may request an administrative stay of removal from the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) while any waiver or provisional waiver application is pending before the USCIS. See 8 C.F.R. § 241.6(a).
 
The respondent has not provided an adequate basis to excuse the untimeliness of the present motion. Nor has he demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant reconsideration of the prior Board decision or reopening of these proceedings in the exercise of discretion under the Board’s sua sponte authority. See, e.g., Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s sua sponte power is limited to exceptional circumstances). Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
