2017 WL 6555096 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SORIE BARRIE
File: AXXX XX1 469 - Atlanta, GA
September 26, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tiana P. Garner, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Sierra Leone, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated May 4, 2017, denying his request for a continuance and ordering his removal from the United States. The Department of Homeland Security has not filed an appellate brief or statement. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2017). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent does not contest on appeal the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that he is subject to removal from the United States under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii) (2017), as an alien who has been convicted of an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(G) and (R) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1101(a)(43)(G) and (R) (IJ at 2; Exh. 1). The respondent also does not challenge the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that he is not eligible at this time for any form of relief available in these proceedings. (IJ at 2). Those issues are not before us.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s speculative pursuit of post-conviction relief in the state jurisdiction where he was convicted of removable criminal offenses did not amount to good cause warranting a further continuance of proceedings (IJ at 2). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. On the date of the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent did not assert that he had even begun the process of applying for post-conviction relief (Tr. at 6-8). In the respondent’s appellate brief, while he does state that his convictions are “in the process of being re-opened,” he does not specifically claim to have filed any specific motion for post-conviction relief with any court. See Respondent’s Br. at 2. In any event, even if the respondent had filed a proper motion in state court seeking relief from the conviction, the respondent’s conviction would remain final for immigration purposes. See Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). While we acknowledge that the respondent may intend to seek, or continue to seek collateral relief, his arguments on appeal do not persuade this Board that his quest for collateral relief is more than speculative.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent makes a claim of ineffective assistance of former counsel in immigration proceedings. We note that the respondent has not shown that he was substantially prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his request for a continuance in order to further challenge his criminal convictions. See Cole v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 712 F.3d 517, 534-35 (11th Cir. 2013); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354, 356 (BIA 1983). Since the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent has not presented any evidence that his convictions were vacated or that he has filed for post-conviction relief.1 For that reason, and because the collateral challenge remains speculative, the respondent’s assertion that former counsel failed to adequately present a case for a continuance is not a basis for a finding of ineffective assistance of counsel. As this Board noted in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d sub nom. Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), a showing of some level of prejudice is a requirement for a successful ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Id. at 638. Finally, the respondent has not articulated on appeal any form of relief for which he is eligible, and therefore his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel cannot be based on an assertion that former counsel improperly failed to apply for relief.2
 
If the respondent believes that he should be permitted to remain in the United States while he further challenges his criminal convictions, he is not precluded from pursuing immigration benefits, such as a stay of removal, directly from the DHS. See 8 C.F.R. § 241.6. However, as we decline to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision to order his removal from the United States, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Statements of counsel made on appeal are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 505-06 (BIA 1980). Furthermore, the copy of the plea colloquy in the respondent’s criminal case that has been submitted in conjunction with this appeal is not, without more, evidence of an ongoing collateral challenge to the convictions.

2

The respondent’s appellate argument that this appeal should be sustained based on the ineffective assistance of counsel in the respondent’s criminal proceedings is unavailing. The proper forum for that claim is with the state court having jurisdiction over the respondent’s claim.

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent’s motion, which has not been opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) is denied. This case was last before the Board on March 21, 2017, when the Board denied the respondent’s untimely filed motion to reopen. The Board concluded that the respondent did not demonstrate that he exercised due diligence during his more than 17-year delay in raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel nor shown that reopening is warranted in the exercise of discretion. The respondent now timely requests reconsideration of that Board decision. However, the respondent’s motion sets forth no adequate basis for reconsideration. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); see also Matter of 0-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (discussing the requirements for motions to reconsider). The respondent’s motion does not identify any prior argument presented that was overlooked by the Board nor does it identify any error of law or fact in the Board’s March 21, 2017, decision. We separately note that the respondent has yet to offer a coherent and persuasive explanation for his extremely lengthy delay in raising a claim against former counsel.
 
We do not find the respondent’s asserted disagreement with the outcome of the Board’s March 21, 2017, decision sufficient to demonstrate either that the Board improperly evaluated or disregarded the facts and evidence presented or committed legal error. The motion to reconsider is therefore denied.1
 
Molly Kendall Clark

2017 WL 5377589 (BIA)
 
Unpublished
  
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HATIM JAMIL ZAKAR
File: AXXX XX1 033 - Detroit, MI
September 22, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dalia R. Kejbou, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jason Ritter
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Iraq, appeals the Immigration Judge’s November 21, 2016, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the Immigration Judge’s factual findings for clear error, including findings as to the credibility of testimony. We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of immigration judges de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
The respondent was admitted to the United States on June 18, 1970, as a lawful permanent resident (IJ at 1; Exh. 1). On February 5, 1991, the respondent was convicted of conspiracy for the “Unlawful manufacture, delivery, or possession with intent to manufacture or deliver . . .” cocaine in the amount of 650 grams or more, in violation of Mich. Comp. Laws. § 333.7401(2)(a)(1), and was sentenced to life in prison without parole (IJ at 1-2). See February 5, 1991, Judgment.1 The respondent was released from custody after serving 19 years in prison (IJ at 2). The respondent was placed in removal proceedings and charged with being removable based on his conviction for an aggravated felony and a controlled substance violation. See sections 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) and 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act; see also Moncrieffe v. Holder, 569 U.S. 184 (2013). On May 1, 2009, the respondent signed a Stipulated Request for Removal Order and Waiver of Hearing in which he said that he had no fear of returning to Iraq (IJ at 2). See Stipulated Removal Request at 2; see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.25(b). The Department of Homeland Security concurred in the respondent’s request for a stipulated removal order.
 
In the Biographic Information form that the respondent completed, however, he indicated that he was afraid “of being harmed and persecuted if returned to Iraq” (IJ at 2). See May 6, 2009, Biographic Information at 2. Because of the inconsistency in the respondent’s statements about his fear of returning to Iraq, the Immigration Judge denied the joint request for a stipulated order of removal and scheduled a master calendar hearing (IJ at 2). See Immigration Judge’s May 14, 2009, Order.
 
At the respondent’s May 18, 2009, hearing, the Immigration Judge informed the respondent that, because of his conviction, he was only eligible to apply for relief under the Convention Against Torture (IJ at 3; Tr. at 2-3). The respondent said that he understood (IJ at 3; Tr. at 3, 4). The respondent declined the Immigration Judge’s offer to reschedule the hearing so that the respondent could speak to his family and potentially obtain counsel (IJ at 3; Tr. at 4). The respondent indicated that he had been in jail for 19 years because of his criminal conviction and that he continued to be detained on an “““immigration hold” (Tr. at 2). The respondent stated that, although he had a fear of returning to Iraq, he did not want to seek relief from removal because he did not want to be detained any longer (Tr. at 1-2, 3-4). The respondent said that he did not “want to get deported, but like I told you, I just want to get out of jail. And if that’s what it takes, to deport me, then that’s your decision, sir.” (IJ at 3; Tr. at 4). The respondent indicated that he understood that he was waiving his right to apply for relief and that he would be ordered removed (Tr. at 4-5). After questioning the respondent about whether he understood his rights, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s request for the entry of a removal order and the respondent waived his right to appeal (IJ at 3; Tr. at 4-5).
 
*2 Seven years later, on October 7, 2016, the respondent filed a Motion to Reopen his removal proceedings. In the motion, the respondent argued that his stipulated removal request was not knowing, voluntary, and intelligent. The respondent asked that his proceedings be reopened so that he could apply for withholding of removal and relief under section 212(c) of the Act. On November 21, 2016, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent now appeals that decision.
 
“A motion to reopen [or remand] proceedings shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). In addition, to obtain reopening, the respondent must make a prima facie showing that he is statutorily eligible for the relief sought and that he warrants relief in the exercise of discretion. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 323 (1992); INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 106 (1988). Prima facie eligibility is shown where the evidence reveals a reasonable likelihood that the statutory requirements for relief have been satisfied and that there is a reasonable likelihood that relief will be granted in the exercise of discretion. See Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413, 419 (BIA 1996).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that his “consent to the joint stipulated request was not knowing, voluntary, considered and intelligent.” See Respondent’s Brief at 1. Contrary to the respondent’s argument, however, the Immigration Judge did not accept the parties’ stipulated removal order. See Immigration Judge’s May 14, 2009, Order. Because the respondent indicated in the Biographic Information that he feared persecution in Iraq, the Immigration Judge held a master calendar hearing and informed the respondent of his rights including to have a hearing and to seek counsel (IJ at 2; Tr. at 1, 2). The Immigration Judge informed the respondent that, because of his conviction, he was only eligible to apply for protection under the Convention Against Torture (IJ at 3; Tr. at 2-3). As noted above, the respondent indicated that he had been in jail for 19 years because of his criminal conviction and that he continued to be detained on an “immigration hold” (Tr. at 2). The respondent stated that he did not want to seek relief from removal because he did not want to be detained any longer. The respondent said that he did not “want to get deported, but like I told you, I just want to get out of jail. And if that’s what it takes, to deport me, then that’s your decision, sir.” (IJ at 3; Tr. at 4). The respondent also said that he understood that he was waiving his right to apply for relief and that he would be ordered removed. After questioning the respondent about whether he understood his rights, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s request for the entry of a removal order (Tr. at 5). Therefore, contrary to the respondent’s argument on appeal, the Immigration Judge did not order the respondent removed pursuant to the stipulated removal order.2
 
*3 In any event, even if the respondent’s waiver was not knowing and voluntary, the respondent sought reopening in order to apply for withholding of removal and relief under section 212(c) of the Act. Motion to Reopen at 3, 5. Nonetheless, he did not present sufficient evidence to show that he was prima facie eligible for such relief. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. at 323; see also Mapouya v. Gonzales, 487 F.3d 396, 415-16 (6th Cir. 2007) (due process claim cannot succeed without a showing that the alleged error materially affected the outcome of his case). As the Immigration Judge found, because the respondent’s conviction for conspiracy to possess with intent to deliver 650 grams or more of cocaine constitutes a particularly serious crime, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for withholding of removal (IJ at 12). See section 241(b)(3)(B)(ii) of the Act; see also Matter of Y-L-, 23 I&N Dec. 270, 274 (A.G. 2002) (convictions for drug trafficking crimes are presumptively convictions for particularly serious crimes under the Act unless the respondent demonstrates “the most extenuating circumstances that are both extraordinary and compelling”); Matter of N-A-M-, 24 I&N Dec. 336, 343 (BIA 2007).3
 
Further, the respondent does not meaningfully challenge on appeal the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent is ineligible for section 212(c) relief because the respondent served 19 years in prison for his aggravated felony conviction (IJ at 11). Matter of Abdelghany, 26 I&N Dec. 254, 272 (BIA 2014) (alien is ineligible for relief under section 212(c) if, inter alia, he has served an aggregate term of imprisonment of at least 5 years as a result of one or more aggravated felony convictions entered between November 29, 1990, and April 24, 1996); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3(f)(4)(i); Respondent’s Brief at 1-2.
 
In addition, the respondent has not made a prima facie showing that it is more likely than not that he would be tortured in Iraq by or with the acquiescence of a public official or other person acting in an official capacity (IJ at 12-13; Tr. at 3). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16(c) and 1208.18(a). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent did not present sufficient evidence to demonstrate that the government of Iraq would acquiesce in the respondent’s torture and the respondent has identified no clear error in that finding (IJ at 12).
 
In sum, given the evidence presented, we uphold the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. at 323 (to obtain reopening, the respondent must make a prima facie showing that he is statutorily eligible for the relief sought and that he warrants relief in the exercise of discretion).
 
*4 Further, while this appeal was pending, the respondent filed a second Motion to Reopen and for Stay of Removal. Under section 207(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act, with limited exceptions, the respondent may file one motion to reopen and it must be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal. See 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). The respondent’s second motion was filed more than 8 years after he was ordered removed, however. Nonetheless, the time and numerical limitations do not apply if the respondent seeks to apply for asylum based on changed country conditions arising in his country of nationality and submits material evidence that was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the previous hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4); see also Matter of C-W-L-, 24 I&N Dec. 346, 349 (BIA 2007).
 
In this case, to demonstrate changed country conditions in Iraq, the respondent relies on news articles indicating that former Secretary of State John Kerry stated in 2014 that actions by ISIS in Iraq constitute genocide against certain minority groups including Christians. See Second Motion to Reopen at 6. Based on these statements, the respondent argues that conditions in Iraq have changed for Christians and, therefore, that he warrants an exception to the time and numerical limitations on the filing of a motion to reopen.
 
Despite this argument, however, the respondent also relied on these news articles when he filed his first Motion to Reopen on October 7, 2016. See First Motion to Reopen at 5. In that motion, the respondent stated that, on March 17, 2016, the “United States government declared that ISIS’s current actions against Christians in Iraq rise to the level of genocide. John Kerry, Sec’y of State.” First Motion to Reopen at 5. The respondent’s second motion to reopen also argues that, based on Secretary Kerry’s statement, the respondent’s removal proceedings should be reopened.
 
Similarly, although the respondent argues that a federal district court judge recently granted a preliminary injunction against the removal of Iraqi nationals from the United States, the respondent’s argument that he fears persecution in Iraq because he is a Christian is substantively the same argument that he made in his first Motion to Reopen. See Second Motion to Reopen at 7, Exh. F.
 
Given the evidence presented, the respondent has not presented material evidence that was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the previous hearing. See Bi Feng Liu v. Holder, 560 F.3d 485, 491 (6th Cir. 2009). For these reasons, we conclude that the respondent has not shown that he qualifies for an exception to the time and numerical limits on the filing of a motion to reopen in order to apply for asylum or withholding of removal.
 
*5 To the extent that the respondent argues that his proceedings should be reopened because his prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance, the respondent did not comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009). To succeed on a motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent should submit an affidavit attesting to the relevant facts including the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken on appeal. Also, the former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed respond. Finally, the motion should reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 639. The respondent must also show that he was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. Sako v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d 857, 863-64 (6th Cir. 2006).
 
In this case, the respondent has not submitted an affidavit attesting to the relevant facts including the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken on appeal, and has not indicated whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not. The respondent also has not shown that he was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. Therefore, because the respondent did not comply with the requirements for filing a motion to reopen to allege ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
Finally, to the extent that the respondent requests a stay of removal pending his appeal, because we dismiss the respondent’s appeal, the request for a stay of removal is moot. To the extent that the respondent argues that his removal should be stayed so that he can apply for relief from removal, the respondent’s request will be construed as a request for reopening and is denied as explained above. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

At the time of the respondent’s conviction, the statute under which the respondent was convicted provided: “Sec. 7401. (1) Except as authorized by this article, a person shall not manufacture, deliver, or possess with intent to manufacture or deliver, a controlled substance . . . . (2) A person who violates this section as to: (a) A controlled substance classified in schedule 1 or 2 which is either a narcotic drug or described in section 7214(a)(iv) and: (i) Which is in an amount of 650 grams or more of any mixture containing that controlled substance is guilty of a felony and shall be imprisoned for life.” Mich. Comp. Laws § 333.7401.

2

The respondent argues that he would have “done anything to get out of custody after serving more than 19 years in prison.” See Respondent’s Brief at 1. This does not demonstrate, however, that the respondent’s acceptance of a removal order was not knowing and voluntary. See Brady v. United States, 397 U.S. 742 (1970). In fact, the respondent concedes that the Immigration Judge advised the respondent of his rights and that “it appears from the record that Respondent may have understood the Immigration Judge[’s] questions.” See Respondent’s Brief at 1.

3

The respondent has not challenged the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent is removable as charged or that his conviction constitutes a particularly serious crime (Tr. at 3-4). See Respondent’s Brief at 1-2.

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: XIOMARA ELOISA HERNANDEZ-PEREZ
File: AXXX XX3 941 - Charlotte, NC
September 20, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Celestine Tatung, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cori White
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 At a hearing held on February 14, 2017, the respondent did not apply for any form of relief and the Immigration Judge ordered her removal to El Salvador. The respondent timely filed a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, asserting that her prior counsel should have pursued an application for asylum and a petition for Special Immigrant Juvenile Status on her behalf. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on April 7, 2017; the respondent’s appeal from that decision will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen, for the reasons set forth in his decision. Even assuming that the respondent complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), she did not establish that her former counsel’s representation was ineffective. The respondent has not demonstrated prima facie eligibility for the relief she contends her former counsel should have sought. The respondent asserts that she entered as an unaccompanied minor and her former counsel should have filed a petition for Special Immigrant Juvenile (SIJ) status (Form I-360) on her behalf. However, the record reflects that the respondent entered the United States at age 19; thus, she did not qualify as an unaccompanied minor, and has not demonstrated that she could have obtained SIJ status.
 
Furthermore, the respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for asylum or withholding of removal. She does not indicate in her motion or on her asylum application1 that she has suffered persecution or fears future persecution on account of an enumerated ground contained in the Act. See Matter of S-P-, 21 I&N Dec. 486 (BIA 1996); sections 208(b)(1)(B)(i) and 241(b)(3)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1158(b)(1)(B)(i), 1231(b)(3)(A). A generalized fear of gang violence does not support a claim for asylum. See Matter of M-E-V-G-, 26 I&N Dec. 227, 235 (BIA 2014) (“asylum and refugee laws do not protect people from general conditions of strife, such as crime and other societal afflictions”); see also Quinteros-Mendoza v. Holder, 556 F.3d 159, 164 (4th Cir. 2009). In light of the foregoing, we find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent did not establish ineffective assistance on the part of her former attorney. Thus, she did not demonstrate that reopening is warranted.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward F. Kelly
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the respondent did not argue or demonstrate a material change in circumstances in El Salvador since her last removal hearing such that reopening is warranted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii).

2017 WL 5377559 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HIRENKUMAR PRAFULCHANDRA PATEL
File: AXXX XX5 584 - Harlingen, TX
September 18, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jaspreet Singh, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jo Ann McLane
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 31, 2017, denying his motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on December 8, 2011. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS), United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS), filed a motion for summary affirmance and argued in opposition to the appeal. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded upon a motion to reopen filed (i) within 180 days after the date of the removal order if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or (ii) at any time if the alien demonstrates that he or she did not receive notice of the hearing in accordance with sections 239(a)(1) or (2) of the Act, or that the alien was in Federal or State custody and did not appear through no fault of the alien. Sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (ii) of the Act; see also Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722, 722-23 (BIA 1999).
 
In this case, the respondent was properly served a Notice to Appear in person on February 7, 2011, while he was in DHS custody at the Willacy Detention Center. While the respondent was detained, an attorney filed a Notice of Entry of Attorney or Representative, Form EOIR-28, on February 11, 2011. The Form EOIR-28 provided the address for the respondent’s attorney of record. On March 8, 2011, the Immigration Court sent a notice of hearing to the address provided by the respondent’s attorney of record (EOIR-28, dated February 11, 2011). The notice of hearing informed the respondent that a hearing was scheduled for December 8, 2011. The notice of hearing was not returned to the Immigration Court as undeliverable.
 
The respondent failed to appear and he was ordered removed in absentia December 8, 2011. If an alien fails to provide an address pursuant to section 239(a)(1)(F) of the Act, then “no notice shall be required.” Section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Act. See Gomez-Palacios v. Holder, 560 F.3d 354, 359-60 (5th Cir. 2009) (court found that alien did not receive notice of his hearing because he failed to comply with his obligation to provide the Immigration Court with current address information).
 
*2 We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that the respondent is properly charged with receipt of the February 11, 2011, notice of the hearing, which scheduled a hearing for December 8, 2011 (IJ at 2). In his motion to reopen, the respondent argues that he filed an Alien Change of Address form, EOIR-33, dated March 8, 2011, and did not receive notice of his hearing held on December 8, 2011 (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2-5). Assuming that the change of address form was filed on March 8, 2011, we note that the change of address was filed after the February 11, 2011, notice of hearing was sent to the attorney of record. Service of the notice of hearing to the attorney of record constitutes adequate notice of the hearing scheduled for December 8, 2011. C.F.R. §§ 1003.13 (defining “Service”), 1295.5(a) (stating that whenever a person is required to be given notice, such notice shall be required to be served upon the attorney or representative or the person if himself if not represented). Moreover, we conclude that the respondent’s motion did not succeed in rebutting the presumption of delivery of the hearing notice in his case. Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674 (BIA 2008). Therefore, the respondent received proper notice of the scheduled hearing.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen filed on May 1, 2017, included an application for asylum (Motion Reopen at 10-20). With certain exceptions, a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on December 8, 2011. Thus, the motion to reopen was untimely by several years. However, the time limits for motions to reopen do not apply if the basis of the motion is to apply or reapply for asylum and for withholding of removal based on changed country conditions arising in the country of nationality, if such evidence is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
In this case, the exception did not apply to the respondent’s motion, for it did not establish changed country conditions in India arising after the issuance of the Immigration Judge’s decision on December 8, 2011 (IJ at 2). Gotora v. Holder, 567 Fed.Appx. 219 (5th Cir. 2014) (“In determining whether evidence accompanying a motion to reopen demonstrates a material change in country conditions, the BIA ‘compare[s] the evidence of country conditions submitted with the motion to those that existed at the time of the merits hearing below.”DD’). The respondent’s motion to reopen included an application for asylum and for withholding of removal but did not present any evidence or arguments to establish that “changed circumstances arising in the country of nationality,” occurring after his hearing in December 2011 show that material changed circumstances or country conditions in India (IJ at 2). Matter of S-Y-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 247, 252 (BIA 2007), aff’d., Shao v. Mukasey, 546 F.3d 138, 171 (2d Cir. 2008). Therefore, the respondent’s motion for reopening for the purpose of applying for asylum and for withholding of removal was properly denied.
 
*3 The respondent claims that his prior counsel did not inform him of the hearing in 2011. However, the respondent has not demonstrated a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in the instant motion. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims before the Board). The respondent has not complied with any of Lozada’s procedural requirements. See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 498 (5th Cir. 2000) (finding that “the general application of the Lozada rules is not an abuse of discretion.”); Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222-23 (11th Cir.2003). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 5377559 (BIA)
End of Document

2017 WL 4946911 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE ENRIQUE GONZALEZ-ROMERO A.K.A JOSE ENRIQUE-GONZALEZ A.K.A. SANTOS RAUL PAZ
File: AXXX XX2 441 - San Antonio, TX
September 15, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gautam J. Jagannath, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ricardo Cuellar
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATIONS: Reopening; change of venue
 
*1 This case was last before us on October 20, 2000, when we entered a final administrative order dismissing the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of his prior motion to reopen. On April 26, 2017, the respondent submitted the instant motion to reopen pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion. The motion exceeds both the time and number limitations for motions to reopen, and it will be denied. The respondent’s request for a change of venue will also be denied.
 
An alien may file only one motion to reopen and, with certain exceptions, it shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); section 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229a(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i). In certain circumstances, an alien may toll the time limit applicable to his motion to reopen by demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In addition to complying with the procedural requirements for such a claim, the alien must show prejudice as a result of the ineffective assistance and that he exercised due diligence during the period he seeks to toll. See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497-498 (5th Cir. 2000); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of El Salvador. On February 4, 1999, he failed to appear for his removal hearing and he was ordered to be removed in proceedings conducted in absentia. On March 30, 1999, he sought to have his proceedings reopened based on a claim that he did not receive proper notice of his hearing. He now seeks reopening based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent relates that he asked the advice of his mother’s attorney, who advised him not to attend his hearing because it was likely that he would be deported. See Motion, ¶ 6; Exhibit 3, ¶¶ 11, 15, 38. He states that the attorney advised him that despite a deportation order, he would be protected by his Temporary Protected Status (TPS). See Motion, ¶ 7; Exhibit 3, ¶ 18. The respondent claims that he did not become aware until 2006 that he had been ordered in 1999 to be removed, and did not understand how serious it was until 2016. See Motion, ¶ 8; Exhibit 3, ¶ 20. The respondent declares that he was unaware that a motion on his behalf had been filed in 1999, and that he found out in 2016, that the attorney who had previously advised him had filed a motion, a declaration, and an appeal on his behalf, but did not timely submit an appeal brief which resulted in the dismissal of the appeal. See Motion, ¶¶ 9-10, 12; Exhibit 3, ¶¶ 21-24. The respondent states that in 2001, he employed that attorney to help him in applying for TPS status on his own, and in 2003, employed him to help him apply for lawful permanent resident status. See Motion, ¶ 11; Exhibit 3, ¶¶ 25-30. He claims that his proceedings should be reopened based on the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Motion, ¶ 16; § III.
 
*2 The respondent also seeks reopening based on claims that his prior application for asylum was withdrawn without his apparent consent, that he is eligible for adjustment of status based on an approved visa petition filed on his behalf by his lawful permanent resident mother, that he is eligible for asylum based on past persecution that he suffered in El Salvador and a fear of persecution there as a member of two particular social groups, as a member of his mother’s family and as a person with tattoos, and that he is eligible for a nonimmigrant U visa petition as a victim of a crime in the United States. See Motion, § IV. However, these claims are not mentioned in the respondent’s affidavit, and statements of counsel are not evidence. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980).
 
The respondent offers his affidavit, his prior motion to reopen, the Immigration Judge’s order denying his motion, the appeal briefing schedule, an extension request grant, our prior order dismissing his appeal, an approval notice for his application for employment authorization, an approval notice for a visa petition submitted on his behalf by his lawful permanent resident mother, a notice of administrative termination of an asylum application in his name but with a different alien registration number, his new application for asylum, an incident report from the Oakland Police Department, a letter to the attorney regarding his allegations of ineffective assistance, his complaint against him filed with the Executive Office of Immigration Review, and his current counsel’s declaration.
 
The respondent admits that he was aware of his hearing scheduled for February 4, 1999, and that he decided not to appear because he was advised by an attorney that he may be deported if he were to appear. We find that the respondent has not demonstrated exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear.
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s claim at this time that he suffered ineffective assistance of counsel because an attorney filed a motion to reopen, a declaration, and an appeal on his behalf in 1999 without his knowledge, and that he did not authorize that attorney to represent him or take such actions. The record reflects that on March 31, 1999, the respondent through counsel submitted a motion to reopen accompanied by a Form EOIR-26, Notice of Appearance as Attorney or Representative, signed by the respondent for representation before the Immigration Judge. The record further reflects that on July 9, 1999, the respondent through counsel submitted an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen accompanied by a Form EOIR-27, Notice of Appearance as Attorney or Representative, signed by the respondent for representation before this Board. We are not convinced that the respondent had not authorized the attorney to act on his behalf or to take those actions.
 
*3 We will deny the respondent’s motion because he has not established that he suffered ineffective assistance of counsel and that he exercised due diligence in raising his claim against his former counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 637; Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. at 561; also Rodriguez-Manzano, 666 F.3d 948, 953 (5th Cir. 2012); Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162 (5th Cir. 2006) (upholding the Lozada requirements). The respondent states that he became aware in 2006 that he had been ordered to be removed based on his failure to appear for his hearing. We find that his claim that he did not understand the seriousness of the situation until 2016 is inadequate to explain his failure to seek reopening or to investigate the actions taken in his case, particularly in light of his history of employing the same attorney between 2001 and 2003. We conclude that equitable tolling of the time limit for a motion to reopen is not warranted in this case.
 
The burden of proof on a motion to reopen is on the alien to establish eligibility for the requested relief. See Altamirano-Lopez v. Gonzales, 435 F.3d 547, 549 (5th Cir. 2006); Zhang v. Gonzales, 432 F.3d 339, 344-45 (5th Cir. 2005). The evidence that the respondent offers is not sufficient to support his claim that he is prima facie eligible for adjustment of status, asylum, and a nonimmigrant U visa petition to warrant reopening his removal proceedings. His motion is not supported by applications for such relief other than an application for asylum. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (a motion to reopen must be accompanied by an appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation). Moreover, the respondent’s motion has not been timely filed, and a claim of eligibility for adjustment of status is generally not a circumstance warranting the grant of an untimely motion to reopen. See Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103, 105 (BIA 2009).
 
The respondent’s motion does not satisfy any of the exceptions to the time limit imposed on motions to reopen removal proceedings, and does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997). We conclude that the respondent has not satisfied his heavy burden of proving that his removal proceedings should be reopened. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992). Accordingly, as the motion exceeds both the time and number limits for motions to reopen, it will be denied. The respondent’s request for a change of venue will also be denied.
 
*4 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen and request for a change of venue are denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 4946911 (BIA)
End of Document

2017 WL 4946918 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DIONICIO VELAZQUEZ MORALES A.K.A. DIONISIO VELASQUEZ-MORALES
File: AXXX XX7 528 - Phoenix, AZ
September 14, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rene Siqueiros, Esquire
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated February 3, 2017, denying the respondent’s request for a further continuance and granting him voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b). We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, de novo. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Initially, we note that the respondent has not submitted a brief in support of his appeal, despite indicating that he would submit a brief and despite requesting and being granted an extension for submission of a brief. Thus, we will consider only the limited statements presented in the respondent’s Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s request for a continuance. The decision to grant a continuance is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge, if good cause is shown, and that decision will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing. Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6.
 
The record reflects that the Immigration Judge continued these proceedings four times between April 2002 and February 2017, to provide the respondent opportunities to seek legal representation and for case preparation. At the February 3, 2017, hearing, the respondent appeared with prior counsel; however, he did not identify any relief from removal that he wished to pursue. Rather, the respondent’s prior counsel made a motion to withdraw from the respondent’s case, and further moved to substitute a different attorney to represent the respondent. The Immigration Judge denied those motions.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that he was provided ineffective assistance of counsel by his prior attorney, and that such ineffective assistance prevented him from pursuing relief available to him. However, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) so as to establish an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Nor has the respondent articulated on appeal any relief for which he may be eligible. Although the respondent asserted that he “may” be eligible for asylum because he was a police officer in Mexico, he has not submitted an application for asylum on appeal, or otherwise set forth a persecution claim. Accordingly, he has not established that any deficient assistance by prior counsel caused him any prejudice. See Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241 (9th Cir. 2000) (requiring a showing of prejudice to establish a violation of due process). Nor has he established that continued proceedings were warranted. The following orders will therefore be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty as provided by the regulations and the statute and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
WARNING: If the respondent files a motion to reopen or reconsider prior to the expiration of the voluntary departure period set forth above, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated; the period allowed for voluntary departure is not stayed, tolled, or extended. If the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated upon the filing of a motion, the penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(e)(1).
 
WARNING: If, prior to departing the United States, the respondent files any judicial challenge to this administratively final order, such as a petition for review pursuant to section 242 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252, the grant of voluntary departure is automatically terminated, and the alternate order of removal shall immediately take effect. However, if the respondent files a petition for review and then departs the United States within 30 days of such filing, the respondent will not be deemed to have departed under an order of removal if the alien provides to the DHS such evidence of his or her departure that the Immigration and Customs Enforcement Field Office Director of the DHS may require and provides evidence DHS deems sufficient that he or she has remained outside of the United States. The penalties for failure to depart under section 240B(d) of the Act shall not apply to an alien who files a petition for review, notwithstanding any period of time that he or she remains in the United States while the petition for review is pending. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(i).
 
*3 Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 4946918 (BIA)

2017 WL 4946930 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SLOBODAN RASKOVIC
File: AXXX XX7 088 - Chicago, IL
September 13, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alexander C. Vrbanoff, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Geoffrey P. Gilpin
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before us on April 18, 2012, at which time we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s March 17, 2011, denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen his in absentia proceedings. The respondent has now filed, on July 31, 2017, an untimely motion to reopen proceedings based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that we should reopen proceedings based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel that he received from the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Court and this Board. Specifically, the respondent argues that his attorney failed to properly request information from him about his mental state, and that as a result, he did not have an opportunity to present the true reasons for his absence from his February 16, 2011, hearing during his previous motion. In support of his current claim, the respondent has submitted, inter alia, medical records and a March 26, 2017, psychological assessment, as well as an affidavit, letters of support, and evidence indicating that he filed a complaint with the Illinois bar in compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Notwithstanding the respondent’s complaint with the Supreme Court of Illinois, Attorney Registration and Disciplinary Commission, he has failed to carry his burden of demonstrating that he suffered ineffective assistance of counsel. The Illinois Disciplinary Commission concluded that they would be “““unable to establish by the requisite standard of evidence that (prior counsel) neglected your legal matter or that he otherwise engaged in professional misconduct.” Moreover, we note that the respondent’s letter to prior counsel, dated May 1, 2017, claims that in preparation for his initial 2011 motion to reopen, “the only questions you asked me were about why I had failed to appear at the hearing.” Under the circumstances, we do not find that the respondent has carried his burden of demonstrating that he suffered ineffective assistance of counsel. Id.
 
Moreover, as the DHS argues in its response brief, the respondent has not brought this motion with due diligence. See El-Gazawy v. Holder, 690 F.3d 852, 859-60 (7th Cir. 2012). The respondent does not assert that he was unaware of the denial of his 2011 motion to reopen proceedings, and he has not demonstrated that he could not reasonably have filed this motion within the last 5 years. Id; see also Patel v. Gonzalez, 442 F.3d 1011 (7th Cir. 2006).
 
*2 Accordingly, we find no merit to the respondent’s motion, and no reason to except the respondent’s motion from the timeliness requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion will be, accordingly, denied as untimely.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 4946930 (BIA)

2017 WL 4946937 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

IN RE: FARHAN MERCHANT
File: AXXX XX7 127 - Dallas, TX
September 11, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William Chu, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Roslyn Gonzalez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Remand; reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent, a native of the United Arab Emirates and a citizen of India, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 20, 2016, ordering the respondent removed from the United States.1 The respondent has also filed a motion to remand.2 The Department of Homeland (DHS) opposes the appeal and the motion. The record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent was placed in removal proceedings pursuant to a Notice to Appear (NTA) filed with the Immigration Court on April 21, 2014 (Exh. 1). At a hearing on May 14, 2015, the respondent, through counsel, admitted the first three allegations in the NTA but denied that he entered into a marriage with a United States citizen for the sole purpose of obtaining an immigration benefit (Tr. at 2). At that time, the respondent also denied the charge of removability under section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A) (Tr. at 2-3). On May 27, 2016, the DHS filed a Form I-261, Additional Charges of Removability, charging the respondent under section 237(a)(3)(D) of the Act (Exh. 3).
 
At a hearing on June 20, 2016, the respondent admitted the previously denied allegation, admitted the allegations contained in the Form I-261, and conceded both charges of removability (Tr. at 8-11). The respondent’s counsel requested a continuance to prepare an application for cancellation of removal for certain nonpermanent residents under section 240A(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1) (Tr. at 13-14). The DHS opposed the respondent’s request, arguing that the respondent is statutorily ineligible for cancellation of removal by virtue of having departed the United States for an aggregate period of more than 180 days (Tr. at 17-18). The DHS separately argued that the respondent was precluded from establishing that he has been a person of good moral character by virtue of his admissions and concessions (Tr. at 18). The respondent’s counsel indicated that the respondent was not seeking any other form of relief (Tr. at 24-25). The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed from the United States pursuant to the charges.
 
*2 As an initial matter, we recognize that the DHS, in its appeal brief, requested reconsideration of the Board’s February 2, 2017, decision exercising jurisdiction over the respondent’s appeal (DHS Br. at 3-4). However, this request is contained within the DHS’s appeal brief and is not a properly filed motion to reconsider. In any event, we would deny the motion as untimely as it was not filed within 30 days of our February 2, 2017, decision and the DHS has not adequately explained the delay in filing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2).
 
The respondent alleges ineffective assistance of former counsel and seeks a remand pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). He argues, inter alia, that his former counsel’s decision to concede the charges of removability constituted ineffective assistance. To support a claim for ineffective assistance, an alien must (1) provide an affidavit attesting to the relevant facts, including a statement of the terms of the attorney-client agreement; (2) inform prior counsel of the allegations and allow prior counsel an opportunity to respond; and (3) file or explain why a grievance has not been filed with the disciplinary authorities against the offending attorney. Id. In addition to these requirements, an alien alleging ineffective assistance of counsel must also show that he or she was prejudiced by the actions or inactions of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. at 640; Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
The respondent has complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada. The evidence offered in support of his motion includes an affidavit from the respondent, a copy of a letter sent to former counsel informing him of the allegations, and a copy of the grievance form that was filed with the State Bar of Texas.
 
Upon consideration of the evidence, we conclude that the respondent has established that he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s ineffective assistance. In general, an alien will be bound by formal admissions and concessions made by counsel acting in his professional capacity. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (holding that the concession of deportability was a reasonable tactical decision by counsel). However, a concession by counsel may constitute ineffective assistance where there is no plausible explanation for how the strategy resulted in a possible tactical advantage. See Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 166 (5th Cir. 2006) (finding ineffective assistance where counsel conceded a false claim of citizenship charge, thereby cutting off all available avenues of relief, without any apparent benefit to the alien). We cannot agree with the DHS’s argument that respondent’s former counsel made a tactical decision to concede the charges to prevent the DHS from presenting witness testimony that would have negatively impacted the outcome of any discretionary form of relief, particularly where the respondent’s former counsel only requested one form of relief, which was unavailable to the respondent for other reasons (DHS Br. at 19).
 
*3 In light of the foregoing, we will grant the respondent’s motion to remand for a hearing on the charges of removability and any other proceedings the Immigration Judge deems appropriate.3 Because the record will be remanded, we do not address the respondent’s remaining arguments. In remanding, we express no opinion on the ultimate outcome of these proceedings. See Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413 (BIA 1996). The following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings and the issuance of a new decision.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

2

The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on July 18, 2016, which was pending at the time he filed this appeal on July 20, 2016. The motion to reopen will be deemed a motion to remand and will be considered in conjunction with the respondent’s appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4).

3

The respondent does not argue that he would be eligible for any form of relief if both charges of removability are sustained.

2017 WL 4946942 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MANUEL JESUS REYES-PARADAS
File: AXXX XX6 929 - San Francisco, CA
September 8, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrew Taylor, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s February 22, 2017, decision denying his request for a further continuance and ordering his removal from the United States. The Immigration Judge declined to continue the respondent’s case so that he could pursue an application for derivative nonimmigrant U-visa relief with United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact, including credibility determinations, under a clearly erroneous standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondent’s request for a continuance in order to file a Petition for Qualifying Family Member of a U-1 Nonimmigrant (Form I-929). See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. In the instant case, the Immigration Judge continued the case twice over the course of one year to provide the respondent with an opportunity to file the Form I-929. At the time of the respondent’s last hearing, however, he did not present evidence of a pending petition; nor did he demonstrate his prima facie eligibility for a derivative nonimmigrant U-visa. See generally Matter of Sanchez-Sosa, 25 I&N Dec. 807 (BIA 2012). On appeal, the respondent’s counsel asserts that he suffers from Attention Deficit Disorder, and that this affects his ability to “timely file.” He asserts that the Immigration Judge was aware of this condition. However, regardless of this appellate assertion, the respondent has not, either before the Immigration Judge, or on appeal, presented evidence that the Form I-929 has been filed. Nor has the respondent asserted that he was provided with ineffective assistance of counsel in these proceedings. Under the circumstances, the Immigration Judge properly denied a further continuance. The respondent has not asserted eligibility for any other relief from removal. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Ana Mann
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 4946942 (BIA)

2017 WL 4946949 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LOURDES LILIANA DIAZ-DIAZ A.K.A. LOURDES LILIANA DIAZ
File: AXXX XX4 627 - Pompano Beach, FL
September 6, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nathaly D. Henriquez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Shaheewa Jarrett Gelin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATIONS: Reopening; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Honduras, appeals the April 14, 2017, decision of the Immigration Judge. In that decision, the Immigration Judge granted the motion of the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) to reconsider the decision of the prior Immigration Judge who had granted the respondent’s motion to reopen her 2007 in absentia removal proceedings. Upon reconsideration, the Immigration Judge denied reopening and ordered the respondent to be removed. The respondent also submits a motion to reopen sua sponte and remand. The DHS opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed and the respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
The record reflects that the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen and remand on May 2, 2017, and that she was removed from the United States on that day. It further reflects that a timely appeal was filed on her behalf on May 9, 2017. We find that we have jurisdiction over the respondent’s appeal despite her removal from the United States. See Matter of Diaz-Garcia, 25 I&N Dec. 794 (BIA 2012) (the Board retains jurisdiction to consider a direct appeal where the removal occurred during the time within which to file an appeal or during the pendency of an appeal). We further find that, as the instant case arises in the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit, we also have jurisdiction over the respondent’s motion to reopen and remand. See Contreras-Rodriguez v. U.S. Attn’y Gen., 462 F.3d 1314 (11th Cir. 2006) (the Immigration Judge and the Board retain jurisdiction to entertain a motion to reopen filed by an alien who has been removed, but who is alleging that he failed to attend his removal hearing due to lack of notice).
 
We review findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of Z-Z-O-, 26 I&N Dec. 586 (BIA 2015; Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We review all other issues under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
*2 The record reflects that the respondent appeared at two hearings, on October 26, 2006, and June 14, 2007, and was personally served with the notice of her following hearings, the next of which was scheduled for August 23, 2007. However, on August 23, 2007, the respondent failed to appear, and she was ordered to be removed in proceedings conducted in absentia.
 
On March 3, 2017, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. She claimed that she attended court and was advised that she would receive notice of the next hearing in the mail, but she never received the notice. She further claimed that she learned in January, 2017, that an in absentia order had been entered in her case, and that her affidavit is sufficient evidence to overcome the presumption of delivery by regular mail.
 
On March 20, 2017, an Immigration Judge found that the respondent was properly notified of the hearing scheduled for August 23, 2007. However, he granted reopening as a matter of discretion to allow the respondent to advise the court of any relief that may be available to her. He also granted a change of venue.
 
At the new venue, an Immigration Judge granted the DHS’ motion for reconsideration. He found that notice of the hearing had been properly served on the respondent, that she made no application for relief from removal, that the evidence did not demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel, and that the respondent did not adequately explain her delay of many years to seek reopening. He concluded that the respondent had not met her heavy burden to demonstrate that reopening was warranted. We affirm his findings.
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge’s decision to grant reopening as a matter of discretion was proper, and that the subsequent decision to reconsider and deny reopening violated her constitutional right to due process because the Immigration Judge lacked jurisdiction or authority to reconsider a decision made by a different Immigration Judge. See Br. at 4-7. We find that the change of venue was proper and that the Immigration Judge at the new venue correctly exercised jurisdiction over the motion for reconsideration.
 
Further, an alien who faces removal is entitled under the constitution to a full and fair hearing. See Matter of D-, 20 I&N Dec. 827, 831 (BIA 1994); Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984); Matter of Exilus, 18 I&N Dec. 276 (BIA 1982). In order to establish that her due process rights were violated, the respondent must prove that there was an error and that she was prejudiced. Id. The respondent has not shown either error or prejudice here.
 
We find no reason to address the respondent’s claim that reopening is warranted because she was preparing to apply for relief based on changed circumstances and a change in the law, including asylum and withholding of removal based on a claim of membership in a particular social group consisting of women who are unable to leave an abusive relationship in her country, protection under the Convention Against Torture, and a petition to the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) for a U non-immigrant visa. See Br. at 7-9. Although the respondent offers an asylum application and a petition for a U non-immigrant visa, she has not shown that they have been filed or demonstrated that she is prima facie eligible for such relief. We conclude that the respondent has not met her heavy burden to establish that reopening is warranted or a reason to remand the record for further proceedings.
 
*3 Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and her motion to reopen and remand will be denied.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and the motion is denied.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 4946949 (BIA)
End of Document

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MUHAMMAD ABDUL FARID A.K.A. MOHAMMAD ABDUL FAREED
File: AXXX XX3 969 - New York, NY
August 25, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sanjay Chaubey, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Pakistan, appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated March 13, 2017, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security has not filed a response to the respondent’s appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact determined by an Immigration Judge, including credibility findings, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2017). We review questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
A motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days after entry of the final administrative decision sought to be reopened. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act. The respondent’s motion to reopen the proceedings more than 10 years after entry of the final order is untimely. The respondent has not shown that any exception to this filing deadline applies.1 See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4).
 
The respondent asserts ineffective assistance of prior counsel. However, the respondent did not comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). The order of removal indicates that the respondent did not file any application for relief. He has not made any specific allegations asserting that his failure to file an application for relief was due to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Sua sponte reopening is reserved for truly exceptional cases. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). “The power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship.” Id. at 984. The respondent has not shown circumstances that qualify as exceptional, and we therefore decline to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen this matter. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (holding that sua sponte authority should be invoked sparingly, “not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). Becoming potentially eligible for relief from removal after the entry of a final order is not, in and of itself, an exceptional circumstance that would justify sua sponte reopening of these proceedings.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen C Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD

1

We note that there is no indication in the Immigration Judge’s October 12, 2006, decision that it was issued after a hearing held in absentia. Moreover, even if it was, any related motion would be untimely unless there was a lack of proper notice. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). This was not alleged in the motion before the Immigration Judge, or otherwise established.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NASIR MEHMOOD
File: AXXX XX8 039 - New York, NY
August 14, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kyce Siddiqi, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Pakistan, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on February 19, 2016, after not appearing at a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on February 15, 2017, and appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 16, 2017, denying the motion. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including issues of law, discretion, or judgment, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that an “exceptional circumstance,” to wit, ineffective assistance of counsel prevented his appearance at the hearing. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i) (stating that a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel must be filed within 180 days of the order of removal); Chen v. Gonzales, 437 F.3d 267, 268 (2d Cir. 2006) (setting forth the requirements for equitable tolling); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel). According to the respondent, he received a Notice of Hearing, dated February 13, 2015, from the Immigration Court, which instructed him to appear on November 29, 2019. He states that he took the notice to his former counsel, and counsel did not instruct the respondent to notify the Immigration Court of any changes in his address. The respondent contends that counsel’s failure to notify him of his obligation to inform the Immigration Court of changes to his address resulted in the respondent not receiving notice for his February 19, 2016, hearing.
 
However, the February 13, 2015, Notice of Hearing informed the respondent of his obligation to update the Immigration Court with his most current address. In addition, according to former counsel’s February 14, 2017, letter to current counsel, he provided the respondent with a copy of the Notice to Appear on September 24, 2014, which advises the respondent of his obligation to report his current address to the Immigration Court. In light of the foregoing, the respondent has not established that the ineffective assistance of former counsel was the cause of his nonappearance at his hearing. It follows that we decline to equitably toll the 180-day period to file a motion to reopen based on “exceptional circumstances,” find the motion to be untimely filed, and we need not address whether the respondent satisfied the procedural requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward F. Kelly
FOR THE BOARD

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANA MONROY INTERIANO A.K.A. ANA ISABEL MONROY INTERIANO A.K.A. ANA MONROY-INTERIANO
File: AXXX XX8 299 - Seattle, WA
August 11, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lesley Irizarry-Hougan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brent D. Campbell
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative decision on December 15, 2015, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s written decision denying a previous motion to reopen these proceedings in which the respondent was ordered removed in absentia on May 19, 2015, under section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5). The respondent again seeks reopening, averring that prior counsel deficiently represented her by not adequately advising her of her immigration hearing date and filing a deficient motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
In her July 24, 2015, declaration supporting her prior motion the respondent averred that she was “confused” about her court date and asked her prior counsel’s office to confirm the date, “but they did not have any information” (Respondent’s Mot. to Reconsider, October 6, 2015, at 21). We will not revisit our finding that this declaration - coupled with the other evidence supporting the previous motion - did not constitute exceptional circumstances to warrant rescission of her in absentia removal order, as a motion to reopen is not an opportunity to repeat previously-considered arguments. See INS v. Wang, 450 U.S. 139, 141 (1981) (discussing motions to reopen); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991); see generally sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and (e)(1) of the Act.
 
The respondent has now submitted a second declaration, claiming that a secretary from her prior counsel’s office informed her and her daughter in a May 18, 2015, telephone conversation that: “[Y]ou don’t have court tomorrow, until you get a new notice” (Respondent’s Mot. to Reopen, February 23, 2017, tab B at 19). The respondent has not adequately explained why her July 24, 2015, declaration omits the allegation that her prior counsel’s secretary provided erroneous information concerning her hearing date. Rather, the respondent waited until February 23, 2017 - well over a year after her October 6, 2015, motion - to raise this allegation for the first time. Under the circumstances, the respondent has not demonstrated that she diligently pursued her ineffective assistance of counsel claim to equitably toll the time and number limits on motions to reopen. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (observing that equitable tolling is predicated on alien identifying and pursuing an ineffective assistance claim with due diligence); Bataev v. Mukasey, 276 F. App’x. 581 (9th Cir. 2008) (affirming Board’s finding that alien did not act with due diligence, where he waited over 1 year to raise ineffective assistance of counsel claim).
 
*2 Nor has the respondent shown prejudice, where she acknowledges receiving the Immigration Court’s notice explicitly informing her of the May 19, 2015, hearing (Respondent’s Mot. to Reopen, February 23, 2017, tab B at 19). Irrespective of prior counsel’s secretary’s purportedly erroneous information, the respondent was aware that her immigration hearing was May 19, 2015. See Martinez-Hernandez v. Holder, 778 F.3d 1086, 1088 (9th Cir. 2015) (an alien can show prejudice where counsel’s performance may have impacted the outcome of the case).
 
Finally, the respondent has not demonstrated an exceptional circumstance warranting sua sponte reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
John Guendelsberger

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
