2017 WL 2376448 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN PABLO FIGUEROA MUNOZ
File: AXXX XX6 793 - Buffalo, NY
April 28, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David L. Wilson, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s October 24, 2016, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed a response to the respondent’s appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
An Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia on September 27, 2013, because he failed to appear for the hearing. On July 27, 2016, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and rescind the September 27, 2013, removal order. The DHS stated that it did not oppose the respondent’s motion.1 On October 24, 2016, the Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, finding that the notice of hearing was sent to the respondent’s address in Minnesota, and the respondent did not rebut the presumption that he received the notice (I.J. at 3). In addition, the Immigration Judge found that the evidence indicates that the respondent chose not to attend the hearing and that he does not have a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel because he cannot show that he suffered prejudice as a result of his former attorneys’ actions (I.J. at 3-4).
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should have reopened the proceedings because the DHS did not oppose the motion. He also argues that he has shown ineffective assistance of counsel, prejudice by his former attorneys’ actions, and that he exercised due diligence so that the filing deadline for his motion to reopen should be equitably tolled. The respondent further argues that the Board should reopen the proceedings under its sua sponte authority.
 
An in absentia removal order may be rescinded only if the alien files a motion to reopen: (1) within 180 days after the date of the removal order in which he establishes that his failure to appear was because of “exceptional circumstances,” as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act; or (2) at any time in which he establishes that he did not have proper notice of the hearing in which the removal order was entered. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent received notice of his September 27, 2013, hearing because the hearing notice was mailed to his address in Minnesota, which he provided upon his release from custody (I.J. at 3). The respondent has not rebutted the presumption that he received notice because the notice of hearing was properly addressed and sent by regular mail according to normal office procedures (I.J. at 3). We discern no clear error in this finding. While the respondent claims on appeal that he did not know about the hearing date and that his attorney did not notify him, the record shows that the hearing notice was sent directly to him and that his attorney had not filed a notice of appearance with the Immigration Court at the time the hearing notice was sent (I.J. at 2-3).
 
*2 Thus, because the respondent had notice of his hearing he can seek reopening of the proceedings solely upon the basis of exceptional circumstances and only within 180 days of the removal order. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The respondent’s motion is therefore untimely because it was not filed within 180 days of the in absentia removal order. Moreover, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not shown ineffective assistance of counsel resulting in prejudice and thus cannot show exceptional circumstances. First, we discern no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that the evidence of record shows that the respondent’s prior attorneys did not properly file a notice of appearance before his September 27, 2013, hearing (I.J. at 2). In addition, the respondent’s affidavit submitted with a June 3, 2014, motion to rescind states that he chose not to attend the hearing because he lived far away and could not afford to make the trip to New York, among other reasons (I.J. at 3-4; Motion to Rescind at Exhibit K, page 62). The respondent argues on appeal that he did not understand the contents of the affidavit, but as the Immigration Judge found, he did not totally disavow the affidavit (I.J. at 4). Moreover, the record shows that the respondent’s prior attorney called the Immigration Court on September 26, 2013, stating that the respondent would not be attending the hearing the next day (I.J. at 4). While the respondent argues that his prior attorney acted incompetently by not filing a motion for change of venue, as the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent knew about the hearing and was still required to appear even if a motion for change of venue had been filed (I.J. at 4 n.5). Because the respondent has not shown prejudice from his prior attorney’s actions he cannot make out an ineffective assistance of counsel claim.2
 
The respondent further argues that even if he has not met the regulatory requirements for rescinding the in absentia order, the Board should reopen the proceedings because the DHS did not oppose his motion to reopen and rescind. However, to prevail on a motion to rescind an in absentia order of removal, the respondent must meet the strict requirements outlined in the regulation. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii); see Alrefae v. Chertoff, 471 F.3d 353, 357 (2d Cir. 2006). As discussed above, the respondent has not shown lack of notice of the hearing date or exceptional circumstances for failing to appear.
 
Moreover, we find that the respondent has failed to establish that reopening is warranted pursuant to our sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The respondent’s motion does not establish an “exceptional” situation sufficient to warrant reopening of his removal proceedings. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen or reconsider cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional situations and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship).
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On November 4, 2016, the respondent and the DHS filed a joint motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order. The Immigration Judge denied the joint motion on November 10, 2016. That decision is not the subject of this appeal.

2

Even if the respondent showed ineffective assistance of counsel, he has not shown that he exercised due diligence during the entire period of time, in excess of 2 years, that he seeks to toll the deadline for filing the motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134 (2d Cir. 2000); Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127, 132 (2d Cir. 2008) (alien must demonstrate due diligence during the entire period he or she seeks to toll, which includes both the period of time before the ineffective assistance of counsel was or should have been discovered and the period from that point until the motion to reopen is filed). The respondent did not hire a new attorney and file the current motion to reopen for more than 2 years after he repeatedly tried to reach his prior attorneys with no response from them. See Respondent’s July 27, 2016, Motion to Reopen at Attachments A and B.

IN RE: JOSE AMABLE NUNEZ-RODRIGUEZ A.K.A. JOSE A. NUNEZ A.K.A. JOSE NUNEZ
File: AXXX XX1 066 - New York, NY
April 25, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George A. Terezakis, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)] - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(E)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(E)(i)] - Convicted of crime of domestic violence, stalking, or child abuse, child neglect, or child abandonment
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)] - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
 
 
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings; waiver of inadmissibility; adjustment of status; continuance; voluntary departure
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 1, 2016, decision ordering him removed from the United States. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
The Immigration Judge has not prepared a separate oral or written decision. Instead, despite the respondent’s denials, the Immigration Judge summarily concluded during the proceedings that the respondent is removable as charged (Tr. at 10-12). The Immigration Judge also decided issues ranging from the merits of a motion to terminate, the respondent’s eligibility for adjustment of status, the request for a continuance, and the respondent’s eligibility for voluntary departure during the proceedings and without providing any factual and/or legal analyses to support her decisions. Her findings on these issues are scattered throughout the record. We find that a separate oral or written decision is necessary in this case. See Matter of A-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 468 (BIA 1999); Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994).
 
In particular, we note that, prior to the Immigration Judge’s decision in this case, the United States Supreme Court issued its decision Mathis v. United States, 136 S. Ct. 2243 (2016), which provides the framework for the analyses necessary to determine the respondent’s removability as charged. Further, the Board decided Matter of Silva-Trevino III, 26 I&N Dec. 826 (BIA 2016), regarding the proper framework for determining whether a conviction is for a crime involving moral turpitude, which also affects the respondent’s removability as charged. Additionally, without any explanations of the Immigration Judge’s findings on the issues of, inter alia, termination, adjustment of status, continuances, and voluntary departure, we cannot properly address the respondent’s appellate arguments. This case does not represent one in which a summarily issued decision should be issued as it is inadequate given the complexity of the issues raised and the applications submitted.
 
*2 Upon remand, the Immigration Judge should apply the correct standards to the respondent’s removability based on his convictions and provide explanations for her findings on the various forms of relief and applications in this case. The Immigration Judge should also consider the arguments raised by the respondent on appeal regarding, inter alia, his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Finally, the Immigration Judge should prepare and issue a separate written decision setting forth her decision and the legal and factual analyses supporting that decision.
 
Accordingly, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHANSA KAPIJIMPANGA
File: AXXX XX0 530 - Dallas, TX
April 17, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lori B. Schoenberg, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on April 28, 2014, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s December 12, 2013, decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia order of removal. The respondent now appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 19, 2016, decision denying his third motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia removal order. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
In our April 28, 2014, decision, we found the respondent is collaterally estopped from seeking to relitigate both his lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel arguments which were raised in his first motion and resolved against him in a final order from which no appeal was taken. See Amrollah v. Napolitano, 710 F.3d 568, 571 (5th Cir. 2013) (“A final decision by an immigration judge has a preclusive effect on future litigation and agency decisions.”). We further found that, because these issues have been finally resolved against him, the respondent’s in absentia removal order can be rescinded and proceedings reopened only “upon a motion filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal [he] demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances” beyond his control. See sections 240(b)(5)(C)(i) and 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i) and 1229a(e)(1). Because the respondent’s motion was filed more than 4 years after his in absentia order was entered, we found it to be untimely, and we dismissed his appeal.
 
On November 16, 2016, the respondent filed a third untimely motion to reopen and rescind his in absentia order with the Immigration Judge. In his decision, the Immigration Judge noted again that the respondent is collaterally estopped from relitigating both his lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel claims, which he seeks to do in his current motion (I.J. at 1). See Amrollah v. Napolitano, supra. The Immigration Judge also found that even if the respondent’s current motion is not number-barred and subject to collateral estoppel, he did not establish he has been pursuing his rights diligently and/or some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way preventing him from timely filing such that the deadline should be equitably tolled (I.J. at 2). See Lugo-Resendez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 337, 344 (5th Cir. 2016). As to the respondent’s claim that he has previously unavailable evidence to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), the Immigration Judge noted that the evidence could have and should have been submitted with the respondent’s first motion (I.J. at 2). Therefore, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion.
 
*2 An in absentia order may be rescinded only if: (1) a motion to reopen is filed within 180 days of the date of the final order and the alien demonstrates there were exceptional circumstances excusing his failure to appear; or (2) a motion to reopen is filed at any time and the alien can demonstrate that failure to appear was due to a lack of proper notice. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (2017). As we found in our previous decision, the respondent is collaterally estopped from relitigating the issues of lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel. See Amrollah v. Napolitano, supra. Further, the respondent’s current motion is both time and number-barred.
 
Contrary to the respondent’s appellate assertions, the Immigration Judge did consider the issue of whether the motions deadline should be equitably tolled (I.J. at 2). See Mata v. Lynch, 135 S.Ct. 2150 (2015). In Lugo-Resendez, the Fifth Circuit instructed that the Board to should determine whether the respondent established: (1) that he has been pursuing his rights diligently, and (2) that some extraordinary circumstance stood in his way and prevented timely filing, such that equitable tolling applies. See id. (internal citations omitted). The first element requires the respondent to establish he pursued his rights with “reasonable diligence,” not “maximum feasible diligence.” See id. (internal citations omitted). The second element requires the respondent to establish an “extraordinary circumstance beyond his control” prevented him from complying with the applicable deadline. See id. (internal citations omitted). The Fifth Circuit also directed that we must consider the individual facts and circumstances of each case in determining whether equitable tolling is appropriate. See id. at 344-45 (internal citations omitted).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that, based on the facts in this case, the respondent has not shown the required due diligence (I.J. at 2). See id. Specifically, the respondent has not shown he has been pursuing his rights diligently or that some extraordinary circumstance prevented him from timely filing. See id. The respondent was first ordered removed in January 2010 and was thereafter detained in November 2012, but he did not follow-up with his prior counsel, whom he now makes ineffective assistance of counsel claims against, during that period of time (I.J. at 2). It was only after the respondent was detained in 2012 that he took significant action (I.J. at 2). Further, the respondent did not file the instant motion until 2 years after his petition for review was summarily dismissed by the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit (I.J. at 2). See id. The respondent’s arguments on appeal are lengthy but not persuasive. The respondent’s motion is time and number-barred, subject to collateral estoppel pertaining to his lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel arguments, and he has not established that equitable tolling is warranted. See id.
 
*3 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2017 WL 1951511 (BIA)

2017 WL 1951512 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HAK BYONG KIM GYUM HWA KIM A.K.A. GYUM HWA JANG HAN BIT KIM KYU JUNG KIM
Files: AXXX XX2 691 - Seattle, WA
AXXX XX7 727
AXXX XX7 728
AXXX XX7 729
April 17, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Frederick P.S. Whang, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Hana A. Sato
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on June 8, 2016, when we dismissed the respondents’ appeal. On December 15, 2016, more than 6 months after the Board’s order, the respondents filed a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondents’ motion is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the Board’s final administrative decision. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondents argue, however, that their proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Steve Martin, who represented the lead respondent1 in 2005 for his adjustment of status application before the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) and who also represented the respondents during parts of these removal proceedings.
 
Specifically, the respondents argue that attorney Martin provided ineffective assistance because: (1) after the respondent retained him in September 2005 for the respondent’s adjustment of status application before the USCIS, he failed to advise the respondent that he should renew his employment authorization, which caused the respondent to continue in an unauthorized employment after his employment authorization expired in November 2006, which in turn resulted in the denial of the respondent’s application for adjustment of status by the USCIS; (2) after the Board dismissed the respondents’ appeal on June 8, 2016, he failed to advise the respondents that they could file a petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit within 30 days of the Board’s decision; and (3) he relocated his law office from the State of Washington to the State of Kansas in or about 2014, and did not notify the respondents of his move (Motion, at 4-7). The respondents submitted the respondent’s statement, communications among their various attorneys, and documents showing that they complied with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), with respect to attorney Martin (Motion Exhs. A-B). We will address the respondents’ arguments in turn.
 
First, as to attorney Martin’s failure to advise the respondent to renew his employment authorization with the USCIS in or about 2006, this was a matter that occurred outside these removal proceedings, and indeed before these removal proceedings were initiated in 2008. As noted by the Ninth Circuit, the extent to which aliens are entitled to effective assistance of counsel during deportation or removal proceedings is governed by the Fifth Amendment due process right to a fair hearing. Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004), amended sub nom. Lara-Torres v. Gonzales, 404 F.3d 1105 (9th Cir. 2005). To establish a due process violation, aliens must show, inter alia, the alleged ineffective assistance rendered “the proceeding . . . so fundamentally unfair that [they were] prevented from reasonably presenting [their] case.” Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, supra (emphasis added); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 894-97 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
*2 In Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, the Ninth Circuit noted that the purported deficiencies of the attorney’s representation in that case did not “““pertain to the actual substance of the hearing (e.g., evidence presented or omitted, arguments raised or overlooked), let alone call the hearing’s fairness into question.” Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, supra. Rather, the substance of the aliens’ claim was that, had it not been for their reliance on the attorney’s defective immigration law advice, they would not have been subject to removal proceedings at all. Id. This “unfairness,” however, did not taint the “““fairness” of the hearing. Id. The Ninth Circuit also noted that “[w]hen we have characterized counsel’s assistance as ineffective, . . . the legal services were rendered while proceedings were ongoing; that is, after a charging document was filed.” Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, supra, at 974 (emphases original). To the extent the respondents’ ineffective assistance of counsel claim against attorney Martin relates to his failure to provide correct advice to the respondent in 2005 or 2006, and such failure did not affect the fairness of these removal proceedings initiated in 2008. Therefore, the respondents have not shown that a reopening of their removal proceedings is warranted based on the ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
In addition, the claimed ineffective assistance by attorney Martin in 2005 or 2006 long predated the respondent’s previous hearing in 2012. Therefore, these are not “new facts” supported by “new evidence” that was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the previous hearing. Section 240(c)(7)(B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Furthermore, the respondents have been represented by different attorneys during the course of these proceedings. After the Immigration Judge ordered the respondents removed on September 23, 2009, the respondents were represented by attorney Michael Piston on appeal and in their motion to reconsider filed in 2011. After the Board granted the motion and remanded the proceedings to the Immigration Judge in 2012, the respondents were again represented by attorney Martin before the Immigration Judge at their October 16, 2012, hearing. However, on their second appeal, the respondents were represented by attorney Michele Redburn, who appears to have been associated with attorney Piston’s law practice. The respondent has not explained why attorney Piston or attorney Redburn could not make the ineffective assistance of counsel claim against attorney Martin. If and to the extent attorney Piston and/or attorney Redburn provided ineffective assistance by failing to make an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against attorney Martin, the respondents have not made valid ineffective assistance of counsel claims against these attorneys by satisfying the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, and they have not explained why they could not do so.
 
*3 Next, as to the respondents’ claim that attorney Martin did not advise them about the Board’s June 8, 2016, decision dismissing their appeal or the possibility of filing a timely petition for review with the Ninth Circuit, as noted above the respondents were represented by attorney Redburn, not attorney Martin, on appeal. Despite the respondents’ argument in the motion and again in their Response to the DHS Opposition to their motion, the respondent admits that he was aware that another attorney was representing the respondents on appeal in 2012 (Motion Exh. A, at 14). Furthermore, although the respondent states that in November 2012 he paid a fee of at least $800 to attorney Martin for his appeal to the Board and considered attorney Martin as his “primary attorney,” the documents submitted show that the respondent made a check of $860 to attorney Piston (Motion Exh. A, at 14, 20), and it was attorney Redburn who represented him on appeal. Despite the respondent’s statement that neither attorney Martin nor attorney Piston contacted him regarding the Board’s June 8, 2016, decision, the documents submitted with the motion do not show what arrangement, if any, was in existence regarding the communication to the respondent following the Board’s decision (Motion Exh. A, at 14). The respondents, as noted above, have not made an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against attorney Redburn, nor did they explain why they cannot do so.
 
In any event, the respondent admits that he did receive a courtesy copy of the Board’s decision on his second appeal in June 2016 (Motion Exh. A, at 14). The cover letter to the courtesy copy received by the respondents, a copy of which was submitted with the motion, clearly informed the respondents that, if the attached decision orders that the respondents be removed or affirms an Immigration Judge’s decision ordering them be removed, “any petition for review of the attached decision must be filed with and received by the appropriate court of appeals within 30 days of the date of the decision” (Motion Exh. B. at 30). The respondent admits that he was aware that he could file a review of the Board’s decision in the event the Board dismissed his appeal, because he and attorney Martin had discussed the possibility of a dismissal by the Board and what the next step would be (Motion, at 14).2 Finally, despite the respondents’ assertion that attorney Martin moved his office to Kansas without informing them (Motion, at 7), the respondent’s statement does not show what difficulty, if any, he had in contacting attorney Martin or any other attorney as a result of that move (Motion Exh. A, at 13-14). Rather, the respondent’s statement shows that he only waited for attorney Martin to contact him (Motion Exh. A, at 14), until he received a call-in letter from the DHS in September 2016.
 
Based on the above, the respondents have not shown that their proceedings should be reopened based on the ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Martin. We also decline to reopen the respondents’ proceedings in the exercise of discretion under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, the respondents’ motion will be denied. The motion for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
*4 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondents are a family of husband, wife, and their two children. The adult male respondent is the lead respondent and principal applicant for relief, and will be referred to as the “respondent” in the singular form in this decision.

2

The respondents’ motion prepared by present counsel states that the respondent was not aware that he could file for further review of the Board’s decision (Motion, at 6-7). However, counsel’s statement in a motion or brief is not evidence. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Furthermore, this assertion is contradicted by the respondent’s own statement, as noted above.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMUEL GOMEZ A.K.A. SAMUEL MARTINEZ GOMEZ A.K.A. SAMUEL MARTINEZ
File: AXXX XX1 930 - Houston, TX
April 6, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raed Gonzalez, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status
 
This case has a lengthy procedural history. On May 17, 2013, an Immigration Judge granted the Department of Homeland Security’s (“DHS”) motion to pretermit the respondent’s application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a).1 In reaching his decision, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent did not meet his burden to establish that he had been lawfully admitted to the United States in 1993 as she claimed.
 
The respondent appealed, and on August 22, 2014, the Board dismissed the appeal finding that the record did not show that the respondent was admitted as a temporary resident in 1993 as he claimed or paroled into the United States as required by section 245(a) of the Act. We also concluded that even if he was admitted in 1993 as a temporary resident, the record reflected that his temporary resident status expired in 1994. We concluded that when the temporary resident status expired, the respondent’s status reverted to that of an alien present in the United States without being admitted or paroled. Our conclusion was based on 8 C.F.R. § 245a.2(u)(4), which provides as follows:
Return to unlawful status after termination. Termination of the status of any alien previously adjusted to lawful temporary residence under section 245A(a) of the Act shall act to return such alien to the unlawful status held prior to the adjustment, and render him or her amenable to exclusion or deportation proceedings under section 236 or 242 of the Act, as appropriate.
 
8 C.F.R. § 245a.2(u)(4).
 
On November 14, 2014, the respondent filed with the Board a timely motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We denied the motion on January 26, 2015.
 
The respondent filed a petition to review our decisions with the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, under whose jurisdiction this case arises. On April 20, 2016, the Fifth Circuit affirmed the Board’s conclusion that the respondent was never lawfully admitted and denied the petition for review. Gomez v. Lynch, 819 F.3d 769 (5th Cir. 2016), superseded by 831 F.3d 652 (5th Cir. 2016).2
 
*2 After that decision, the respondent and the DHS filed with the Fifth Circuit a joint motion to vacate because the DHS located evidence confirming that the respondent had been admitted into the United States in 1993 as the respondent had previously claimed. The Fifth Circuit granted the motion to vacate its April 20, 2016, opinion and accepted briefing from the parties on the interpretation of 8 C.F.R. § 245a.2(u)(4).
 
The case is now before the Board pursuant to the Fifth Circuit’s August 5, 2016, remand order. Gomez v. Lynch, 831 F.3d 652 (5th Cir. 2016). The court agreed with the respondent that his 1993 admission, which occurred while he held temporary resident status, was not affected by his unlawful status in the United States. Id. at 658, 662.
 
The court reasoned that status and admission are fundamentally different concepts. Id. at 658-60. Citing to Matter of Quinlantan, 25 I&N Dec. 285 (BIA 2010), the court noted that an admission is defined in wholly factual and procedural terms. An alien “who presents himself at an immigration checkpoint, undergoes a procedurally regular inspection, and is given permission to enter the United States has been admitted, regardless of whether he had any underlying legal right to do so.” Id. at 658. The court explained status as the type of permission to be present in the United States. The Fifth Circuit stated that “[because] the concepts are separate, an alien can be admitted despite the absence of any lawful status, as when an immigration officer accidentally processes him through a checkpoint.” Id. at 658-59. Conversely, “an alien who was admitted in a lawful status can acquire unlawful status, as by overstaying a visa - but that does not mean he was never admitted.” Id. at 659.
 
The court also explained that section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a) includes the admission/status distinction by providing separate bars to adjustment of status with different exceptions and is addressed in different subsections of the Act. Finally, the court explained that the regulatory language of 8 C.F.R. § 245a.2(u)(4) that provides for an alien to return to a previous unlawful status does not undo a factual admission that occurred during the time when the alien was in lawful status. Id. at 662.
 
Thus, although the respondent returned to his previous unlawful status when his temporary resident status expired, his lack of status did not undo his historical, factual admission for purposes of adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(a) of the Act. We conclude that a remand is warranted for the Immigration Judge to consider the respondent’s application for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act. On remand the parties should have the opportunity to update the record regarding the respondent’s application.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The Board’s August 22, 2014, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge issued a prior decision on September 19, 2012, to this effect but the Immigration Judge gave the respondent additional time to identify any other relief for which he may have been eligible. In his May 17, 2013, decision, the Immigration Judge entered his final order granting the DHS’s motion to pretermit (I.J. dated May 17, 2013, at 2).

2

The court also affirmed the denial of the motion to reopen.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLOS RAFAEL DELCID-CRUZ
File: AXXX XX1 673 - Los Fresnos, TX
March 31, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Luther Snavely, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Fair hearing
 
*1 On December 5, 2016, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and granted him voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b), in lieu of removal. The respondent waived appeal. On January 4, 2017, the respondent filed a Notice of Appeal in which he asserts that his former attorney’s handling of his case deprived him of his right to due process, and he seeks remand to the Immigration Judge to enable him to apply for relief from removal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Generally, the decision of an Immigration Judge becomes final immediately upon the waiver of an alien’s right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction to adjudicate a case in which the alien has waived the right to appeal. See Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993). The record reveals that the Immigration Judge’s decision was personally served on the respondent and his attorney on December 5, 2016. The respondent has not set forth an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board). Further, the respondent does not claim that the waiver of the appeal was not knowing and voluntary. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320, 1322-23 (BIA 2000). Under the circumstances, we will dismiss the appeal for lack of jurisdiction and return the record to the Immigration Judge. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Judge.
 
Roger Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 In a decision dated April 13, 2015, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable under section 237(a)(1)(D)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i) (alien admitted for permanent residence on a conditions basis whose status was terminated); denied his request for a waiver under section 216(c)(4)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1186a(c)(4)(B) (From I-751); and denied his application for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, 1229c(b).1 The Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision on September 13, 2016. On December 12, 2016, the respondent filed the instant timely motion to reopen the proceedings to enable him to apply for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a), based on a pending visa petition filed by his wife, who is United States citizen. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security has not filed an opposition to this motion. The motion will be granted.
 
The evidence offered in support of the motion includes documentation showing that that the respondent and his current wife are the parents of two United States citizen children. Upon consideration, the motion to reopen will be granted.2 See Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). Although we have affirmed the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent did not show that he entered his prior marriage in good faith, we cannot conclude that this automatically bars him from adjustment under section 204(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1154(c).
 
The following orders will be entered.3
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Molly Kendall Clark

IN RE: WILLIAM KIHARA KIRIMA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Shirley C. Zambrano, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case was last before us on January 23, 2017, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and denied his motion to remand proceedings based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reconsider this decision on February 22, 2017. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent has not advanced any specific argument regarding any error in our prior decision, and we find no error in law or fact, or argument that was overlooked, that would warrant further consideration of his claim. See generally Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). The respondent argues that the Board erred in finding that he had not substantially complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), but we find no error in this conclusion (Bd. Dec. at 1-2). With his motion, the respondent has now filed various notarized statements as well as evidence that he filed a grievance against his prior attorney with the State Bar of Georgia. Cast as a motion to reopen, the respondent has not adequately demonstrated that such evidence “was not available and could not have been discovered or presented” with his appeal and motion to remand. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Moreover, the respondent has presented no additional evidence that would alter our previous conclusion that the respondent has not been prejudiced by any action or inaction by his former counsel (Bd. Dec. at 2). Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984). The additional statements the respondent has now submitted are not a basis for reconsideration or reopening.1
 
Given the above findings, we also find no reason to disturb our previous rejection of the respondent’s due process claim (I.J. at 2). We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s or this Board’s reliance on counsel’s indication that the respondent had admitted the allegations and conceded removability (Tr. at 9). See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). As such, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that although the respondent has asserted that he did not have time to discuss his sexual orientation with counsel and did not know it could be a basis for an asylum claim, he has also stated that he did not disclose the information because he had not told his parents about it.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JORDANA ALMEIDA-ABADIA
File: AXXX XX8 852 - Atlanta, GA
March 30, 2017
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eli A. Echols, Esquire
*1 A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was mailed on January 11, 2017. The appeal was accordingly due on or before February 10, 2017. The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was mailed after the due date and filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on February 21, 2017. The respondent asks that the late appeal be accepted because her prior attorney failed to inform her of the date of the Immigration Judge’s decision and of her right to appeal. However, the respondent provides no details or evidence to support this claim, nor has she made an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We find that the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is accordingly now final, and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
Because we are dismissing the appeal as untimely, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See sections 240(c)(6)(A) & (B) and 240(c)(7)(A) & (C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(6)(A) & (B) and (c)(7)(A) & (C); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 In light of the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
